Denying the Cause: First Response to Naqd
Author presents the first of five answers to naqd: arguing the cause is not actually present.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and formulate the 'denial of the cause' (man') response when faced with a naqd objection in a dialectical argument.

Core Idea
Naqd fails if the cause is not actually present in the counterexample.

When a disputant raises an objection by citing a case where the ruling is absent despite the apparent cause, there is a standard first defence: challenge the existence of the cause itself. This lesson unpacks this dialectical move, using the fiqh example of a father killing his son.

Guided Path
1
Identify the naqd objection
Find the counterexample that seems to break the causal link. In the passage, the objection is: intentional murder (cause) leads to qisas, but a father who kills his son is not executed.
The objector claims this shows the cause is invalid.
2
Understand the man' defence
The defender (here the Maliki) responds by denying that the cause is present in the counterexample. The father's act may be discipline, not murder, so the cause does not exist.
This is not a claim of falsehood but lack of evidence for the cause's existence.
3
Examine the example closely
Analyse the father-son case: why does the Maliki say the cause is absent? Because the intention (qatl 'amd) is not proven due to the presumption of compassion.
The defender uses qara'in (circumstantial evidence) to negate the cause.
4
Assess the dialectical impact
If the cause is absent, the absence of the ruling is expected, not a defect. This actually confirms the in'ikas (co-extensiveness) of the cause.
The objection backfires: it becomes evidence for the cause's validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى أنه قادح فالجواب عنه من خمسة أوجه : الأول : منها منعُ وجود الوصف الذي هو علة الحكم في صورة النقض فيصير تخلف الحكم لعدم وجود علته، فلا نقض إذا . ومثاله أن يقال فيما لو رمى الوالد ولده بحديدة مثلاً فقتله : قتل عمد عدوان، وهو علة القصاص، وقد تخلف حكمها عنها في هذه الصورة وهو القصاص؛ لأن الوالد لا يُقتل بولده مع وجود علة الحكم التي هي القتل عمدًا عدوانًا . فيقول المالكي ومن وافقه : العلة غير موجودة في هذه الصورة؛ فعدم القصاص فيها لعدم وجود العلة، ففيها دليل على صحة انعكاس العلة الذي هو عدم الحكم عند عدمها، بل رمي الوالد لولده بحديدة أو نحوها يَحتمل أن يقصد به التأديب؛ لقرينة شدة شفقة الأب، ولا يقصد به القتل، فلم يتحقق وجود العلة التي هي القتل العمد العدوان في هذه الصورة، ومرادنا مطلق المثال لا مناقشة أدلة الأقوال .
And on the view that it is a defect, the answer to it is from five aspects: The first: among them is denying the existence of the description which is the cause of the ruling in the case of naqd, so the absence of the ruling becomes due to the absence of its cause, so there is no naqd then. Its example is what is said about a father who throws an iron piece at his son and kills him: intentional killing with transgression, and it is the cause of qisas, and its ruling has been absent in this case, namely qisas, because the father is not killed for his son despite the existence of the cause of the ruling which is intentional killing with transgression. So the Maliki and those who agree with him say: The cause is not present in this case; so the absence of qisas in it is due to the absence of the cause, so it contains evidence of the correctness of the reflection of the cause, which is the absence of the ruling when it is absent. Rather, the father's throwing an iron piece at his son or similar could be intended for discipline, due to the indication of the father's strong compassion, and he does not intend to kill, so the existence of the cause which is intentional killing with transgression has not been realized in this case. And we mean the absolute example, not a critique of the evidence for the positions.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents the first of five answers to the objection of naqd when it is considered a defect. The answer is to deny that the cause (the legal effective reason) actually exists in the supposed counterexample. If the cause is not present, then the absence of the ruling is not a valid criticism. The author illustrates with a well-known example from Islamic law: a father kills his son by throwing a piece of iron. The general rule is that intentional murder (qatl 'amd wa 'udwan) is the cause for qisas (retribution). However, in this case, the father is not executed for killing his son, despite the apparent presence of the cause. The Maliki school and those who agree argue that the cause is not truly present because the father's act may have been intended as discipline, not murder, given the strong compassion of a father. Therefore, the absence of qisas is due to the absence of the cause, not a defect in the cause itself. This actually confirms the soundness of the reciprocal implication (in'ikas) of the cause: when the cause is absent, the ruling is absent. The author notes that this is just an example and not a detailed discussion of the evidence for different positions. This is a fundamental dialectical technique: to refute an objection by questioning whether the alleged cause is truly present in the objection scenario.

المنع
الانعكاس
القصاص

Key Takeaway
The first answer to naqd is to deny that the cause exists in the counterexample, turning the objection into confirmation of the cause's co-extensiveness.

Denying the Cause: First Response to Naqd
Author explains the first response to naqd: denying the existence of the description (cause) in the counter-example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a dialectical response that denies the existence of the alleged cause in a naqd counter-example, using the example of a father's killing of his son.

Core Idea
Denying the cause's existence is a valid dialectical move when the cause is ambiguous.

In legal analogy, an objector may raise a counter-example (naqd) where the ruling is absent despite the apparent presence of the cause. The respondent has five possible answers. The first answer is to deny that the cause (the description) truly exists in that case. This lesson unpacks this strategy using the classic fiqh example of a father who kills his son.

Guided Path
1
Identify the naqd objection
In the example, the objector says: the cause (intentional unjust killing) is present, but the ruling (qisas) is absent. Extract this claim.
2
Locate the respondent's move
The Malikis deny that the cause exists, arguing that the father's action may be disciplinary, not intended to kill.
3
Analyze the reasoning
They use the principle that the ruling follows the cause; if the cause is absent, the ruling's absence is justified and does not constitute a naqd.
4
Assess the strategy's condition
This response works only when there is plausible doubt about the cause's presence; not in clear cases of intent to kill.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعلم أنه على القول بأن النقض تخصيص لعموم حكم العلة لا نقض لها فلا إشكال فيه، ولا يحتاج إلى جواب ؛ لعدم القدح به على هذا القول .
وعلى أنه قادح فالجواب عنه من خمسة أوجه :
الأول : منها منعُ وجود الوصف الذي هو علة الحكم في صورة النقض فيصير تخلف الحكم لعدم وجود علته ، فلا نقض إذا . ومثاله أن يقال فيما لو رمى الوالد ولده بحديدة مثلاً فقتله : قتل عمد عدوان، وهو علة القصاص، وقد تخلف حكمها عنها في هذه الصورة وهو القصاص ؛ لأن الوالد لا يُقتل بولده مع وجود علة الحكم التي هي القتل عمدًا عدوانًا .
فيقول المالكي ومن وافقه : العلة غير موجودة في هذه الصورة؛ فعدم القصاص فيها لعدم وجود العلة، ففيها دليل على صحة انعكاس العلة الذي هو عدم الحكم عند عدمها ، بل رمي الوالد لولده بحديدة أو نحوها يَحتمل أن يقصد به التأديب؛ لقرينة شدة شفقة الأب، ولا يقصد به القتل، فلم يتحقق وجود العلة التي هي القتل العمد العدوان في هذه الصورة، ومرادنا مطلق المثال لا مناقشة أدلة الأقوال .
الثاني من الأجوبة عن النقض: منع تخلف الحكم عن العلة، بأن يقول : الحكم موجود لوجود علته
ومثاله ما لو أضجع الوالد ولده فذبحه أو شق بطنه أو قطع رأسه، ونحو ذلك مما لا يحتمل التأديب بحال، بل لا يحتمل إلا قصد القتل عمدًا عدونًا، فإنّ المالكية ومن وافقهم يقولون بوجوب القصاص من الأب في نحو هذه الصورة.
فلو قال المعترض عليهم في هذه الصورة قتل عمد عدوان، علة القصاص، وقد تخلف حكمها عنها الذي هو القصاص،
وهذه هي وذلك نقض لها .
فإن المالكية يجيبون عن هذا النقض بمنع تخلف الحكم فيقولون : الحكم غير متخلف هنا والقصاص واجب من الأب في هذه الصورة. ومن الأمثلة أيضًا حرية ولد المغرور ؛ لأن المستدل يجيب بأنّ الحكم موجود، وهو رق الولد ؛ لوجود علته التي هي رق أمه، ولكن ذلك الرقيق فداه أبوه بقيمته وهذا عند من يقول بلزوم القيمة، كما جنح إليه ابن قدامة في روضة الناظر
والمالكية الذين يقولون بوجوب القصاص من الأب في القتل الذي لا يحتمل إلا قصد إزهاق الروح كما مثلنا له يقولون: قولكم (الوالد سبب في وجود الولد فلا يصح أن يكون الولد سببًا في إعدامه) منتقض بما لو زنى الأب بابنته ؛ فإنه يُرجم إجماعا، فقد كان سببا في وجودها وكانت سببًا في إعدامه، وجناية الزنى ليست أعظم من جناية القتل .
Know that according to the view that naqd is a specification of the generality of the ruling of the cause, there is no naqd for it, so there is no problem in it, and it does not need an answer; because there is no refutation with this view. And if it (naqd) is a refutation, then the answer to it is from five aspects: The first: denying the existence of the description that is the cause of the ruling in the case of naqd, so the ruling is absent due to the absence of the cause, so there is no naqd. Its example is the case where a father throws at his son a piece of iron and kills him: it is intentional unjust killing, which is the cause of qisas, but the qisas ruling is absent in this case because the father is not killed for his son, despite the presence of the cause which is intentional unjust killing. So the Maliki and those who agree with him say: the cause does not exist in this case; the absence of qisas is due to the absence of the cause, so it indicates the validity of the converse of the cause, which is the absence of the ruling when the cause is absent. Rather, a father throwing at his son a piece of iron or similar may be intended as discipline, due to evidence of intense paternal compassion, and not intended as killing, so the existence of the cause which is intentional unjust killing is not realized in this case. We only intend a general example, not to debate the evidence of different positions. The second answer: denying the absence of the ruling from the cause, by saying the ruling exists because its cause exists. Its example is if a father lays his son down and cuts his throat, slits his belly, cuts off his head, and so on, which in no way can be interpreted as discipline, but only as intending to kill intentionally and unjustly: the Malikis and those who agree with them say qisas is obligatory from the father in such a case. If the objector says against them in this case: 'intentional unjust killing is the cause of qisas, but the ruling (qisas) is absent', this is a naqd. The Malikis answer this naqd by denying the absence of the ruling, saying: the ruling is not absent here; qisas is obligatory from the father in this case. Also among the examples is the freedom of the child of the deceived spouse (al-maghroor), because the proponent answers that the ruling exists, namely the child is a slave, because its cause exists which is the mother being a slave, but that slave was ransomed by his father with his value, and this is according to those who say the value is obligatory, as inclined to by Ibn Qudama in Rawdat al-Nadhir. The Malikis who say qisas is obligatory from the father in killing that can only intend to end life, as we exemplified, say: your statement 'the father is a cause of the child's existence, so it is not valid for the child to be a cause of his elimination' is refuted by the case of a father committing zina with his daughter, for he is stoned by consensus; he was a cause of her existence and she became a cause of his elimination, and the crime of zina is not greater than the crime of killing.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage from a work on adab al-bahth (jadal) deals with the objection of naqd (counter-example) against the 'illa (cause) in qiyas (analogical reasoning). The author first clarifies that if one holds the view that naqd merely specifies the general ruling (takhsees), then it is not a real objection and needs no answer. But if naqd is considered a refutation (qadih), then there are five possible answers. Here he explains the first two.

The first answer is to deny that the description (al-wasf) which is the cause actually exists in the purported counter-example. The example given is from fiqh: a father throws a piece of iron at his son, killing him. Prima facie, this is intentional unjust killing, which is the cause for qisas (retaliation). But the ruling of qisas is not applied because fathers are not executed for killing their children. The Malikis respond by arguing that the 'illa (intentional unjust killing) is not present because the father's action could be interpreted as discipline due to his paternal compassion, so it is not certain that he intended to kill. Therefore, the absence of qisas is due to the absence of the cause, not a genuine naqd. This actually supports the validity of the converse of the cause (that absence of ruling indicates absence of cause).

The second answer is to deny that the ruling is absent; i.e., the ruling actually applies in the counter-example, so there is no contradiction. The example: a father clearly intends to kill his son (by cutting his throat, etc.), where there is no possibility of discipline. The Malikis assert that qisas is obligatory in such a case. Thus the ruling is present, not absent, so the naqd fails. Another example: the child of a deceived spouse (al-maghroor) — the ruling that the child is a slave exists (because the mother is a slave), but the father ransoms him, so the ruling is not absent; the opponent mistakenly thought it was absent.

The passage then adds a further rebuttal: the principle that 'the father is the cause of the child's existence, so the child cannot be the cause of the father's death' is itself subject to naqd: a father who commits zina with his daughter is stoned to death, so the daughter becomes the cause of his execution. Thus the principle is not absolute.

Throughout, the discussion illustrates the dialectical method of defending an 'illa against counter-examples by either denying the presence of the 'illa or insisting on the presence of the ruling.

Key Terms
النقض
العلة
منع وجود الوصف

A) Because the father intended to kill his son.

C) Because the son is not a valid target for qisas.
D) Because qisas is never applied from a father.

Key Takeaway
The first response to naqd is to deny the existence of the cause in the counter-example, shifting the argument to the presence of the cause.

Denying the Absence of the Ruling: Second Response to Naqd
Author explains the second answer to naqd: denying the absence of the ruling by asserting the ruling actually applies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a dialectical response that denies the absence of the ruling in a naqd objection, using examples from fiqh.

Core Idea
When the cause is clearly present, the respondent must insist the ruling is present, not absent.

The second response to a naqd objection is to assert that the ruling actually applies in the counter-example, so there is no contradiction. This lesson examines this strategy through the example of a father who clearly kills his son and the case of the child of a deceived spouse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the naqd objection
In the second example, the objector says: the cause (intentional unjust killing) is present, but qisas is absent for a father.
2
Locate the respondent's move
The Malikis deny that the ruling is absent, arguing that qisas is obligatory from the father in such a clear killing.
3
Analyze the reasoning
They maintain that the cause is present and the ruling follows; the objector mistakenly thinks it is absent based on a different scenario.
4
Recognize the condition
This response requires that the ruling genuinely applies; it cannot be used if the ruling is indeed absent by consensus.

Know that according to the view that naqd is a specification of the generality of the ruling of the cause, there is no naqd for it, so there is no problem in it, and it does not need an answer; because there is no refutation with this view. And if it (naqd) is a refutation, then the answer to it is from five aspects: The first: denying the existence of the description that is the cause of the ruling in the case of naqd, so the ruling is absent due to the absence of the cause, so there is no naqd. Its example is the case where a father throws at his son a piece of iron and kills him: it is intentional unjust killing, which is the cause of qisas, but the qisas ruling is absent in this case because the father is not killed for his son, despite the presence of the cause which is intentional unjust killing. So the Maliki and those who agree with him say: the cause does not exist in this case; the absence of qisas is due to the absence of the cause, so it indicates the validity of the converse of the cause, which is the absence of the ruling when the cause is absent. Rather, a father throwing at his son a piece of iron or similar may be intended as discipline, due to evidence of intense paternal compassion, and not intended as killing, so the existence of the cause which is intentional unjust killing is not realized in this case. We only intend a general example, not to debate the evidence of different positions. The second answer: denying the absence of the ruling from the cause, by saying the ruling exists because its cause exists. Its example is if a father lays his son down and cuts his throat, slits his belly, cuts off his head, and so on, which in no way can be interpreted as discipline, but only as intending to kill intentionally and unjustly: the Malikis and those who agree with them say qisas is obligatory from the father in such a case. If the objector says against them in this case: 'intentional unjust killing is the cause of qisas, but the ruling (qisas) is absent', this is a naqd. The Malikis answer this naqd by denying the absence of the ruling, saying: the ruling is not absent here; qisas is obligatory from the father in this case. Also among the examples is the freedom of the child of the deceived spouse (al-maghroor), because the proponent answers that the ruling exists, namely the child is a slave, because its cause exists which is the mother being a slave, but that slave was ransomed by his father with his value, and this is according to those who say the value is obligatory, as inclined to by Ibn Qudama in Rawdat al-Nadhir. The Malikis who say qisas is obligatory from the father in killing that can only intend to end life, as we exemplified, say: your statement 'the father is a cause of the child's existence, so it is not valid for the child to be a cause of his elimination' is refuted by the case of a father committing zina with his daughter, for he is stoned by consensus; he was a cause of her existence and she became a cause of his elimination, and the crime of zina is not greater than the crime of killing.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage from a work on adab al-bahth (jadal) deals with the objection of naqd (counter-example) against the 'illa (cause) in qiyas (analogical reasoning). The author first clarifies that if one holds the view that naqd merely specifies the general ruling (takhsees), then it is not a real objection and needs no answer. But if naqd is considered a refutation (qadih), then there are five possible answers. Here he explains the first two.

The first answer is to deny that the description (al-wasf) which is the cause actually exists in the purported counter-example. The example given is from fiqh: a father throws a piece of iron at his son, killing him. Prima facie, this is intentional unjust killing, which is the cause for qisas (retaliation). But the ruling of qisas is not applied because fathers are not executed for killing their children. The Malikis respond by arguing that the 'illa (intentional unjust killing) is not present because the father's action could be interpreted as discipline due to his paternal compassion, so it is not certain that he intended to kill. Therefore, the absence of qisas is due to the absence of the cause, not a genuine naqd. This actually supports the validity of the converse of the cause (that absence of ruling indicates absence of cause).

The second answer is to deny that the ruling is absent; i.e., the ruling actually applies in the counter-example, so there is no contradiction. The example: a father clearly intends to kill his son (by cutting his throat, etc.), where there is no possibility of discipline. The Malikis assert that qisas is obligatory in such a case. Thus the ruling is present, not absent, so the naqd fails. Another example: the child of a deceived spouse (al-maghroor) — the ruling that the child is a slave exists (because the mother is a slave), but the father ransoms him, so the ruling is not absent; the opponent mistakenly thought it was absent.

The passage then adds a further rebuttal: the principle that 'the father is the cause of the child's existence, so the child cannot be the cause of the father's death' is itself subject to naqd: a father who commits zina with his daughter is stoned to death, so the daughter becomes the cause of his execution. Thus the principle is not absolute.

Throughout, the discussion illustrates the dialectical method of defending an 'illa against counter-examples by either denying the presence of the 'illa or insisting on the presence of the ruling.

Key Terms
النقض
العلة
منع تخلف الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The cause is not present, so no ruling.
D) The ruling is suspended pending investigation.

Key Takeaway
The second response to naqd is to assert that the ruling is actually present, denying the objector's assumption that it is absent.

Answering an Objection by Denying the Absence of the Ruling
Author illustrates the first method of responding to a naqd with two examples from Islamic law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the method of 'denying the absence of the ruling' in a jadal exchange and apply its condition to evaluate whether the response is valid.

Core Idea
Denying absence is only valid if you actually hold that ruling exists.

In Islamic dialectic, an objector may attack a causal link by showing a case where the cause exists but the ruling does not. The responder can counter by denying that the ruling is truly absent. This lesson unpacks that method using two legal examples and a crucial condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (naqd) structure
The objector claims: 'Illa is present, but the hukm is absent. This invalidates the causal link between 'illa and hukm.
The naqd is a challenge to the universality of the causal relationship.
2
Extract the responder's answer
The responder asserts: The hukm is actually present in that case. This is the first method of answering a naqd.
For the father killing his child, the Malikis assert that qisas is obligatory.
3
Check the condition
This answer is only valid if the responder himself holds that the hukm applies in that case. If the responder denies the hukm, he cannot use this method.
Consistency is key: the responder must be committed to the presence of the ruling.
4
Apply to the examples
Verify how the Malikis apply this in the father killing case and the mghrar case. In the mghrar case, they argue that the ruling (enslavement) exists but the father ransomed the child.
The existence of the ruling is maintained, even if its practical effect is modified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثاله مالو أضجع الوالد ولده فذبحه أو شق بطنه أو قطع رأسه، ونحو ذلك مما لا يحتمل التأديب بحال، بل لا يحتمل إلا قصد القتل عمدًا عدونًا، فإنّ المالكية ومن وافقهم يقولون بوجوب القصاص من الأب في نحو هذه الصورة.
فلو قال المعترض عليهم في هذه الصورة قتل عمد عدوان، علة القصاص، وقد تخلف حكمها عنها الذي هو القصاص، وهذه هي وذلك نقض لها .
فإن المالكية يجيبون عن هذا النقض بمنع تخلف الحكم فيقولون : الحكم غير متخلف هنا والقصاص واجب من الأب في هذه الصورة. الأمثلة أيضًا حرية ولد المغرور ؛ لأن المستدل يجيب بأنّ الحكم موجود، وهو رق الولد ؛ لوجود علته التي هي رق أمه، ولكن ذلك الرقيق فداه أبوه بقيمته وهذا عند من يقول بلزوم القيمة، كما جنح إليه ابن قدامة في روضة الناظر (١)
والمالكية الذين يقولون بوجوب القصاص من الأب في القتل الذي لا يحتمل إلا قصد إزهاق الروح كما مثلنا له يقولون: قولكم (الوالد سبب في وجود الولد فلا يصح أن يكون الولد سببًا في إعدامه) منتقض بما لو زنى الأب بابنته ؛ فإنه يُرجم إجماعا، فقد كان سببا في وجودها وكانت سببًا في إعدامه، وجناية الزنى ليست أعظم من جناية القتل .
وشرط صحة الجواب بهذا ألا يكون انتفاء الحكم في صورة النقض مذهب المستدل فالذي يرى عدم القصاص من الأب في الصورة المذكورة لا يمكنه أن يجيب عن النقض المذكور بوجود الحكم الذي هو القصاص، لأنه يرى عدم وجوبه، وكذلك الذي يرى عدم لزوم القيمة في ولد المغرور، فلا يمكنه الجواب بهذا.
An example of that is if the father lays down his child and slaughters him, or splits his belly, or cuts off his head, and similar actions that do not admit of discipline at all, but only admit of the intent to kill deliberately and aggressively. Indeed, the Malikis and those who agree with them say that retaliation (qisas) is obligatory from the father in such a case. So if the objector says against them in this case: 'Intentional aggressive killing is the 'illa for qisas, and its ruling, which is qisas, has been absent from it' — this is an objection (naqd) against it. Then the Malikis answer this objection by denying the absence of the ruling, saying: 'The ruling is not absent here; qisas is obligatory from the father in this case.' Also among the examples is the freedom of the child of the deceived (mghrar), because the arguer answers that the ruling exists, namely the enslavement of the child, due to the existence of its 'illa, which is the enslavement of his mother, but that slave was ransomed by his father with its value — this according to those who hold that the value is obligatory, as inclined to by Ibn Qudama in Rawdat al-Nazir. The Malikis who say that qisas is obligatory from the father in a killing that does not admit except the intent to take life, as we exemplified, say: 'Your statement, "The father is a cause of the existence of the child, so the child cannot be a cause of his annihilation," is refuted by the case if the father commits adultery with his daughter; for he is stoned by consensus. He was a cause of her existence, and she was a cause of his annihilation. And the crime of adultery is not greater than the crime of killing.' The condition for the validity of this answer is that the absence of the ruling in the objection's case should not be the doctrine of the arguer. So one who holds that there is no qisas from the father in the mentioned case cannot answer the mentioned objection by asserting the existence of the ruling (qisas), because he holds that it is not obligatory. Similarly, one who holds that the value is not obligatory in the case of the child of the deceived cannot answer with this.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage demonstrates the first method of answering a naqd (objection) in Islamic dialectic (jadal). The objector argues that a known cause ('illa) is present but the ruling (hukm) is absent, which would invalidate the causal relationship. The answerer, here the Malikis, counters by denying the absence of the ruling, asserting that the ruling does apply in that specific case. Two examples illustrate this: first, the father killing his child in a manner that only intends death—the Malikis hold that qisas is obligatory, so the ruling is not absent. Second, the case of the child of a deceived man (mghrar): the ruling of enslavement exists, but the father ransomed the child, so the ruling is effectively present. A condition is then added: this answer is only valid if the opponent himself does not hold that the ruling is absent; otherwise, the answer would be inconsistent with his own doctrine. This shows the importance of consistency in dialectical debate.

Key Terms
نقض (naqd): An objection that points out a case where the 'illa is present but the hukm is absent, intended to refute the causal link. [Note: In later usul al-fiqh, naqd often refers to a specific type of objection in ta'lil.]
علة ('illa): The effective cause or ratio legis that grounds a legal ruling; here, intentional killing is the 'illa for qisas. [Note: In logic, 'illa can mean cause in a broader sense; here it is juristic.]
حكم (hukm): The legal ruling that follows from the 'illa; here qisas or enslavement. [Note: Stable across usul al-fiqh.]
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind for intentional killing or injury; here specifically the death penalty for the father. [Note: Standard fiqh term, no significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) They deny the absence of qisas, asserting that qisas is obligatory in that case.
C) They argue that the 'illa is absent due to a preventative factor.
D) They claim the case is an exception by text.

Key Takeaway
The first method of answering a naqd is to deny the absence of the ruling, but it is only valid if the responder's own doctrine holds that the ruling is present in that case.

Three Additional Methods of Answering an Objection
Author enumerates three more ways to respond to a naqd: preventing cause, exception by text, and competing interests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three additional methods of answering a naqd (objection) in dialectic.

Core Idea
When a counterexample threatens your 'illa, invoke a preventer, an exception, or an outweighing harm.

In the previous lesson, you learned two basic ways to respond to a naqd. Now, the author introduces three more refined strategies: showing a preventer or missing condition, pointing out a textual exception, and appealing to competing interests. These strategies are essential in usul al-fiqh debates to defend causal claims against apparent counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three methods
Read the Arabic passage and locate the three methods: (3) mani' or missing shart, (4) istithna' bi al-nass, (5) ta'arud al-maslaha wa al-mafsada.
2
Define key terms for each method
For method 3, understand that a mani' blocks the 'illa's effect, and a shart is a condition for its operation. For method 4, note that a specific text exempts the case from the general rule. For method 5, grasp that the justice (maslaha) behind the 'illa can be outweighed by a greater harm (mafsada).
3
Apply to the given examples
Match each example to its method: (3) the examples of sale of al-'araya etc. are from earlier; (4) the sale of al-'araya is excepted by text; (5) the compelled person eating carrion shows maslaha outweighed by mafsada. Verify the logical structure: the 'illa is present yet ruling absent, and the method explains why.
4
Test your understanding
Given a new naqd scenario, decide which method is appropriate. For instance, if someone objects that wine is prohibited despite being a painkiller, you might invoke a preventer (harm outweighs benefit).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث من أوجه الجواب عن النقض : بيان وجود مانع من تأثير العلة في الحكم، أو فقد شرط تأثيرها فيه، وقد بيناهما قريبا بمثالهما . الرابع من أوجه الجواب عن النقض : هو كون الصورة الوارد فيها النقض مستثناة من القاعدة الكلية بالنص، كما قدمنا أمثلته ببيع العرايا، وصاع التمر في لبن المصرّاة، وتحمل العاقلة الدية . الخامس من الأجوبة عن النقض : هو أن تكون المصلحة المشتملة عليها العلة معارضة بمفسدة أرجح منها أو مساوية لها . كأن يقال في أكل المضطر الميتة : قذارة الميتة علة لحرمة أكلها، والعلة التي هي قذارتها موجودة في هذه الصورة، مع أن الحكم الذي هو منع الأكل متخلّف عنها . فيجاب عن هذا بأن مصلحة تجنب المستقذرات معارَضَةٌ في هذه الصورة بمفسدة هي أرجح منها، وهي هلاك المضطر إن لم يأكل الميتة، فقذارة الميتة علة لمنع الأكل، ولكنها هنا عُورضت بما هو أقوى منها .
The third of the aspects of answering the objection: showing the existence of a preventative (mani') preventing the 'illa from affecting the ruling, or the absence of a condition (shart) for its effect — we have explained both recently with their examples. The fourth of the aspects of answering the objection: is that the case in which the objection occurs is excepted from the general rule by a text, as we have given examples earlier concerning the sale of al-'araya, the sa' of dates in the milk of al-musarra, and the bearing of the 'aqlah of the blood money. The fifth of the answers to the objection: is that the benefit (maslaha) contained in the 'illa is opposed by a more preponderant or equivalent harm (mafsada). For example, it is said concerning the eating of carrion by the one compelled: 'The filth of carrion is the 'illa for its prohibition, and the 'illa which is its filth is present in this case, yet the ruling, which is the forbiddance of eating, is absent from it.' The answer to this is that the benefit of avoiding filth is opposed in this case by a harm that is more preponderant, namely the destruction of the compelled person if he does not eat the carrion. So the filth of carrion is the 'illa for forbidding eating, but here it is opposed by something stronger than it.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage lists three additional methods to answer a naqd (objection) in dialectic. The third method is to show that a preventative (mani') prevents the 'illa from operating, or that a condition (shart) for its operation is absent. The author says these have been explained earlier. The fourth method is to show that the case in question is excepted from the general rule by a specific textual evidence, citing examples: the sale of al-'araya, the sa' of dates in the milk of al-musarra, and the 'aqlah bearing blood money. The fifth method is to argue that the benefit (maslaha) underlying the 'illa is outweighed or matched by a greater harm (mafsada). An example is given: in the case of a compelled person eating carrion, the 'illa of filth is present, but the harm of death outweighs the benefit of avoiding filth, so the ruling of prohibition is lifted. This passage is an enumeration of standard strategies in usul al-fiqh and jadal for defending a causal claim against counterexamples.

Three Ways to Answer the Naqd in Jadal: Preventive Factors, Textual Exceptions, and Conflicting Interests
Author defines the third, fourth, and fifth methods of answering a logical objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the third, fourth, and fifth methods of answering a logical objection (naqd) in jadal and apply each to a new case from Islamic law.

Core Idea
When the cause is present but the ruling is absent, three responses: a preventive factor, a textual exception, or a stronger opposite interest.

In the previous lesson, we saw two ways to answer a naqd objection: challenging the presence of the cause or denying the ruling. Now, the author introduces three more powerful responses. These methods allow the defender to uphold the cause without abandoning the general rule, by showing that something is blocking the ruling, that the case is an exception by divine text, or that the cause's interest is outweighed by a greater harm. Understanding these tools is essential for sound legal and theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of a naqd objection
In jadal, a naqd (objection) occurs when the opponent points out a case where the claimed cause (illah) is present but the ruling (hukm) is absent, or vice versa. This challenges the validity of the cause. Before examining the answers, ensure you can identify the cause, the ruling, and the case where they conflict.
For example, if the cause 'impurity' is claimed for prohibition, the naqd might point to carrion being impure but permissible for the starving person.
2
Third answer: Show a preventive factor or missing condition
The defender can argue that even though the cause is present, there is a preventive factor (mani') that blocks its effect, or a necessary condition (shart) for its effect is absent. This preserves the general rule while explaining the exception.
The author mentions this has been explained earlier with examples; likely from usul al-fiqh, like a preventive factor blocking the effect of a contract.
3
Fourth answer: Argue a textual exception
The defender can claim that the case in question is excepted from the universal rule by an explicit scriptural text (nass). This is a direct appeal to revelation, bypassing the need for logical causation for that specific case.
Examples given: the 'ariyya sale (exception to riba), the sa' of dates in the confined milk, and the blood-money liability of the 'aqila. These are well-known exceptions in Islamic jurisprudence.
4
Fifth answer: Invoke a conflicting interest (maslahah vs. mafsadah)
The defender can argue that the interest (maslahah) intended by the cause is opposed by a corruption (mafsadah) that is either stronger or equal. In such a case, the ruling is suspended to avoid the greater harm.
Classic example: The impurity of carrion is the cause for prohibition, but for a starving person, the interest of avoiding impurity is outweighed by the corruption of death, so the ruling is lifted.
5
Practice distinguishing the three answers
Given a new scenario where a naqd is raised, identify which of the three answers is most appropriate. For instance, if the opponent says 'Wine is prohibited because it intoxicates, but khamr made from dates is intoxicating yet some permit it', consider which answer might apply.
This step trains you to apply the concepts rather than just memorize them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث من أوجه الجواب عن النقض : بيان وجود مانع من تأثير العلة في الحكم، أو فقد شرط تأثيرها فيه، وقد بيناهما قريبا بمثالهما . الرابع من أوجه الجواب عن النقض : هو كون الصورة الوارد فيها النقض مستثناة من القاعدة الكلية بالنص، كما قدمنا أمثلته ببيع العرايا، وصاع التمر في لبن المصرّاة، وتحمل العاقلة الدية . الخامس من الأجوبة عن النقض : هو أن تكون المصلحة المشتملة عليها العلة معارضة بمفسدة أرجح منها أو مساوية لها . كأن يقال في أكل المضطر الميتة : قذارة الميتة علة لحرمة أكلها، والعلة التي هي قذارتها موجودة في هذه الصورة، مع أن الحكم الذي هو منع الأكل متخلّف عنها . فيجاب عن هذا بأن مصلحة تجنب المستقذرات معارَضَةٌ في هذه الصورة بمفسدة هي أرجح منها، وهي هلاك المضطر إن لم يأكل الميتة، فقذارة الميتة علة لمنع الأكل، ولكنها هنا عُورضت بما هو أقوى منها .
The third of the ways of answering the objection: explaining the existence of a preventive from the effect of the cause upon the ruling, or the absence of a condition for its effect upon it; and we have explained both of them recently with examples. The fourth of the ways of answering the objection: that the case in which the objection occurs is excepted from the universal rule by the text, as we presented examples of it: the sale of 'ariyya, the sa' of dates in the confined milk, and the bearing of the blood money by the 'aqila. The fifth of the answers to the objection: that the interest contained in the cause is opposed by a corruption more preponderant or equal to it. For example, regarding the compelled person eating carrion: the impurity of carrion is the cause for the prohibition of eating it, and the cause which is its impurity is present in this case, while the ruling which is the prevention of eating is absent from it. So this is answered by saying that the interest of avoiding impurities is opposed in this case by a corruption more preponderant than it, which is the destruction of the compelled person if he does not eat the carrion; thus the impurity of carrion is a cause for the prevention of eating, but here it is opposed by what is stronger than it.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage describes three specific methods for answering a logical objection (naqd) in the context of jadal. The third method involves showing that a preventive factor (mani') exists or that a condition (shart) for the cause's effect is missing. The author notes this has been illustrated earlier. The fourth method argues that the case in question is an exception to the general rule by explicit scriptural text, providing examples from Islamic law: the 'ariyya sale, the payment of a sa' of dates in the case of a confined she-camel, and the blood money liability of the 'aqila. The fifth method applies when the interest (maslahah) that the cause (illah) provides is outweighed or equaled by a corruption (mafsadah). The example given: the impurity of carrion is the cause for its prohibition, but in the case of a person compelled by starvation, the interest of avoiding impurity is outweighed by the greater harm of death. Thus the ruling is not effective due to a conflicting interest.

النقض
المانع
الشرط
المصلحة
المفسدة

D) The cause is invalid altogether.

Key Takeaway
A naqd objection can be answered by pointing to a preventive factor, a textual exception, or a conflicting interest that outweighs the cause's intended benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the case: 'Alcohol is prohibited because it intoxicates. However, a certain type of fermented drink is intoxicating but some scholars permit it in small amounts.' Understanding Kasr: The Objection of Defect in Cause in Usul al-Fiqh
Author defines kasr in usul, lists its three forms, and discusses the first form's validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define kasr, list its three forms, and explain the dispute over the first form's validity as an objection.

Core Idea
Kasr reveals that a ruling may exist without its wisdom—or vice versa—challenging the coherence of causality in usul.

In the previous discussion on broken objections in debate, the author introduced the concept of al-naqd al-maksur. Now he connects it to the technical term 'kasr' used in usul al-fiqh. Kasr is a specific type of objection that targets the cause ('illa) by showing a defect in its parts. Understanding kasr is crucial for anyone engaging in legal reasoning, as it tests whether a ruling truly follows from its cause. This lesson will define kasr, enumerate its three forms, and examine a key disagreement about the first form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of kasr
The author defines kasr as showing a defect in some parts of the cause. Note the three forms listed: (1) existence of ruling without wisdom, (2) existence of wisdom without ruling, (3) invalidating part of a compound cause.
Kasr is specifically used in usul al-fiqh, not general logic.
2
Examine each form
Form 1: The ruling is present but its wisdom (hikmah) is absent. Form 2: The wisdom is present but its ruling is absent. Form 3: The objector invalidates one part of a compound cause, and the remaining parts are insufficient for causation, provided the arguer cannot replace the invalidated part.
The third form has a condition about the arguer's inability to substitute.
3
Understand the wisdom (hikmah)
Hikmah is defined as bringing/perfecting benefit or repelling/reducing harm; it is the reason why the attribute became a cause. The example: wine prohibition is ruling, intoxication is cause, preservation of intellect is wisdom.
Wisdom is distinct from the cause itself; it is the underlying purpose.
4
Analyze the dispute over Form 1
Some scholars consider the existence of a ruling without its wisdom as a valid objection (qadih) because the wisdom is absent. Others argue it is not valid because the ruling is attached to the manifestation (mazinnah), not the wisdom itself, so the ruling can remain even without the wisdom.
The disagreement hinges on whether the ruling is tied to the wisdom or to the manifestation.
5
Consider the implications
The dispute leads to different outcomes in subsidiary rulings. The author cites Maraqi al-Su'ud summarizing the known disagreement, and notes that legal rulings built on this principle vary accordingly.
This shows how theoretical disagreement affects practical legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكسر تنبيه : اعلم أن ما قدمنا مما يسميه أهل البحث والمناظرة بالنقض المكسور وأوضحناه بأمثلة وبينا المقبول منه والمردود له شبه في الجملة بالقادح المسمى في اصطلاح أهل الأصول بالكسر . واعلم أن ضابط القادح المسمى في اصطلاح أهل الأصول بالكسر هو أن يبيّن المعترض خللاً في بعض أجزاء العلة، وذلك بثلاثة أمور : الأول : بيانه وجود الحكم دون حكمته . الثاني : عكسه، وهو وجود الحكمة دون حكمها . والثالث : إبطال المعترض بعض أجزاء العلة المركبة، ويكون الباقي من الأجزاء بعد الجزء الذي أبطله ليس صالحًا للتعليل، بشرط المستدل عن الإتيان ببدل صالح للتعليل في مكان الجزء الذي عجز أبطله المعترض . واعلم أولاً أن الحكمة في اصطلاح أهل الأصول هي جلب المصلحة أو تكميلها، أو دفع المفسدة أو تقليلها، وضابطها أنها هي التي صار الوصف علة من أجلها كما قال في تعريفها صاحب مراقي السعود وهي التي من أجلها الوصفُ جرى علةَ حُكم عند كل من درى فتحريم الخمر مثلاً هو الحكم، والإسكار هو علة التحريم، والمحافظة على العقل هي الحكمة؛ لانها هي التي من أجلها صار الإسكار علة لتحريم المسكر، وهكذا . وإذا عرفت ذلك فاعلم أن وجود الحكم بدون حكمته قد قال بعض أهل العلم إنه كسر قادح في العلة؛ لتخلف حكمتها التي صارت من أجلها علة، وقال بعضهم إنه ليس بقادح . ووجه قول من قال إنه قادح ظاهر؛ لتخلف الحكمة التي أساس العلة التي صارت من أجله علة . ومن قال إنه ليس بقادح قال إنه لا يكون إلا في المعلَّل بالمظان، والمعللُ بالمظانّ لا يتخلف فيه الحكم بتخلف الحكمة؛ نظرا إلى إناطة الحكم بالمَظِنّة لا بنفس الحكمة وأشار إلى هذا الخلاف صاحب مراقي السعود بقوله : وفي ثبوت الحكم عند الانتفا للظن والنفي خلافٌ عُرفا والفروع المبنية على هذه القاعدة منها ما يرجح فيه بعض العلماء ثبوت الحكم ؛ بناء على أن هذا النوع من الكسر ليس بقادح، ومنها ما يرجح بعضهم انتفاء الحكم فيه لانتفاء حكمته ؛ بناء على أن هذا النوع من الكسر قادح .
Section on Kasr. Note: Know that what we have previously presented, which the people of research and debate call 'al-naqd al-maksur' (the broken objection) and we clarified it with examples and explained the acceptable and rejected among it, has a general resemblance to the objection called in the terminology of the scholars of usul 'al-kasr' (the breaking). And know that the definition of the objection called in the terminology of the scholars of usul 'al-kasr' is that the objector shows a defect in some parts of the cause, and that is through three matters: First: his showing the existence of the ruling without its wisdom. Second: its opposite, which is the existence of the wisdom without its ruling. Third: the objector's invalidating some parts of the compound cause, and the remaining parts after the part that he invalidated are not suitable for causally attributing, provided that the arguer is unable to bring a suitable replacement in place of the part that the objector invalidated. And know firstly that 'al-hikmah' (wisdom) in the terminology of the scholars of usul is the bringing of benefit or its completion, or the repelling of harm or its reduction, and its definition is that it is that for which the attribute became a cause, as the author of Maraqi al-Su'ud said in defining it: 'It is that for which the attribute proceeds as a cause of a ruling, according to everyone who knows.' So, for example, the prohibition of wine is the ruling, intoxication is the cause of the prohibition, and the preservation of the intellect is the wisdom; because it is that for which intoxication became a cause for the prohibition of intoxicants, and so on. And when you know that, then know that regarding the existence of the ruling without its wisdom: some scholars have said that it is a valid kasr objection against the cause, due to the absence of its wisdom for which it became a cause, and some have said that it is not a valid objection. And the reasoning of those who said it is a valid objection is apparent; due to the absence of the wisdom which is the basis of the cause for which it became a cause. And those who said it is not a valid objection said that it only occurs in cases of 'al-mu'allal bi al-mazann' (the causally attributed based on manifestation), and the causally attributed based on manifestation does not have the ruling absent with the absence of the wisdom; looking to the attachment of the ruling to the manifestation, not to the wisdom itself. And the author of Maraqi al-Su'ud alluded to this disagreement by saying: 'And regarding the establishment of the ruling when it is absent due to probability and its negation, a known disagreement exists.' And the subsidiary issues built upon this rule include those where some scholars give preponderance to the establishment of the ruling, based on the view that this type of kasr is not a valid objection, and those where some scholars give preponderance to the absence of the ruling due to the absence of its wisdom, based on the view that this type of kasr is a valid objection.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the term 'kasr' as used by usul scholars in the context of debate. The author begins by noting a similarity between what was previously called 'al-naqd al-maksur' (broken objection) and the usul term 'kasr'. He then defines 'kasr' as an objection that shows a defect in some part of the cause ('illa), and specifies that it has three forms: (1) existence of the ruling without its wisdom (hikmah), (2) existence of the wisdom without its ruling, and (3) invalidating a part of a compound cause such that the remaining parts are insufficient for causation, provided the arguer cannot provide a suitable replacement. The author then defines 'hikmah' as the bringing or completion of benefit, or the repelling or reduction of harm; it is the purpose for which the attribute became a cause. He gives the example of wine: the ruling is prohibition, the cause is intoxication, and the wisdom is preservation of the intellect. Next, the author presents a dispute: some scholars consider the first form (ruling present without its wisdom) to be a valid objection (qadih) because the wisdom is absent; others disagree, arguing that this occurs only when the ruling is attached to a manifestation (mazinnah) rather than the wisdom itself. The author cites a poetic line from Maraqi al-Su'ud summarizing the disagreement, and notes that the subsidiary legal rulings built on this principle vary accordingly.

Key Takeaway
Kasr is a powerful tool in usul al-fiqh to test causality, but the validity of the first form (ruling without wisdom) is contested, hinging on whether the ruling is attached to manifestation or wisdom itself.

Does Absence of Hikma Invalidate the 'Illa?
Author defines 'illa and hikma, presents two positions, and illustrates with legal examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two positions on the role of hikma in the validity of 'illa and identify which position is being applied in a given legal example from fiqh.

Core Idea
The validity of an 'illa in fiqh depends on whether its underlying wisdom is considered essential or merely indicative.

In this passage, the author presents a critical debate in Islamic legal theory: does the absence of the wisdom (hikma) behind a legal cause ('illa) invalidate that cause? This question has major implications for how rulings are extended to new cases. We will examine both positions and see how they play out in practical examples.

Guided Path
1
Define 'illa and hikma
Identify the definitions given in the passage: 'illa is the attribute that causes a ruling, hikma is the underlying benefit that makes that attribute suitable.
Example: intoxication ('illa) and preservation of intellect (hikma).
2
Extract the two positions
Position A: Absence of hikma is a defect (qaadih) that invalidates the 'illa. Position B: It is not a defect; rulings are based on indicators (mazannah), not the wisdom itself.
The passage states each view clearly.
3
Understand the reasoning
Position A argues that the 'illa exists because of its wisdom, so its absence breaks the causal link. Position B argues that the 'illa is an indicator that usually coincides with the wisdom, so the ruling stands even if the wisdom is absent in a particular case.
This is a fundamental usul al-fiqh dispute.
4
Apply to the travel example
Consider the traveler whose ship covers the distance quickly with no hardship. Position A would deny shortening prayer (wisdom absent), while Position B would allow it (cause exists).
This is a direct application from the passage.
5
Evaluate the branches
List the other examples (istibra' of a young girl, istinja' from dry stone, etc.) and determine which position each supports.
The passage shows that different scholars prefer different positions for different branches.

وهي التي من أجلها الوصفُ جرى علةَ حُكم عند كل من درى علة التحريم، فتحريم الخمر مثلاً هو الحكم، والإسكار هو علة لتحريم المسكر، والمحافظة على العقل هي الحكمة؛ لانها هي التي من أجلها صار الإسكار علة لتحريم المسكر، وهكذا. وإذا عرفت ذلك فاعلم أن وجود الحكم بدون حكمته قد قال بعض أهل العلم إنه كسر قادح في العلة؛ لتخلف حكمتها التي صارت من أجلها علة، وقال بعضهم إنه ليس بقادح . ووجه قول من قال إنه قادح ظاهر؛ لتخلف الحكمة التي أساس العلة التي صارت من أجله علة . ومن قال إنه ليس بقادح قال إنه لا يكون إلا في المعلَّل بالمظان، والمعللُ بالمظانّ لا يتخلف فيه الحكم بتخلف الحكمة؛ نظرا إلى إناطة الحكم بالمَظِنّة لا بنفس الحكمة وأشار إلى هذا الخلاف صاحب مراقي السعود بقوله : وفي ثبوت الحكم عند الانتفا للظن والنفي خلافٌ عُرفا والفروع المبنية على هذه القاعدة منها ما يرجح فيه بعض العلماء ثبوت الحكم ؛ بناء على أن هذا النوع من الكسر ليس بقادح، ومنها ما يرجح بعضهم انتفاء الحكم فيه لانتفاء حكمته ؛ بناء على أن هذا النوع من الكسر قادح . ومن الفروع المبنية على ذلك استبراء الصغيرة؛ لأن تجدّد ملك الأمة علة لاستبرائها، وحكمة الاستبراء هي تحقيق براءة الرحم من الحمل، وهي متحققة في الصغيرة بدون الاستبراء . وكمن مسكنه على البحر ونزل منه في سفينة قطعت به مسافة القصر في لحظة فهل يجوز له قصر الصلاة والفطر في رمضان لوجود علة ذلك وهي السفر، أو لا يجوز له ذلك نظرًا إلى تخلف الحكمة التي هي رفع المشقة ؛ إذ لا مشقة على المذكور أصلاً؟ . ومنها شرع الاستنجاء من خروج حصاة منه لا بلل معها، ووجوب الغسل على النفساء من وضع الولد جافًا لا دم معه . ومنها عدم نقض الوضوء بمس الذكر إذا لم توجد اللذة في اللمس بباطن الكف أو الأصابع . ومنها نقض الوضوء بالقبلة على الفم إذا لم توجد اللذة. ومنها مالو قال لامرأته : أنت طالق مع آخر جزء من الحيض؛ فإنه طلاق صادف الحيض، وهو علة لتحريم الطلاق، ولكن يستعقب العدةَ فلا تطويل فيه، فالحكمة الموجبة للمنع التي هي التطويل منتفية هنا . ومنها ما لو قال : أنت طالق مع آخر جزء من الطهر؛ فإنه عكسُ الصورة المذكورة قبله . فعلى قول من قال إن تخلّف الحكمة عن الحكم قادح في العلة) فلا يجوز ] (١) عنده قصر الصلاة والإفطار في رمضان لمن لم تلحقه
It is that because of which the description runs as the cause of a ruling according to everyone who knows the cause of prohibition. For example, the prohibition of wine is the ruling, and intoxication is the cause for the prohibition of the intoxicant, and the preservation of the intellect is the wisdom; because it is that because of which intoxication became a cause for the prohibition of the intoxicant, and so on. And when you know that, then know that regarding the existence of the ruling without its wisdom, some scholars have said it is a defect that invalidates the cause, due to the absence of its wisdom because of which it became a cause. And some said it is not a defect. The argument of those who said it is a defect is apparent; due to the absence of the wisdom that is the basis of the cause because of which it became a cause. And whoever said it is not a defect said that it only occurs in a ruling based on an indicator, and a ruling based on an indicator does not have its ruling lapse with the lapse of the wisdom; considering that the ruling is attached to the indicator, not to the wisdom itself. The author of Muraqi as-Su'ud alluded to this disagreement with his words: 'And concerning the establishment of the ruling upon its absence, there is a known disagreement regarding the presumption and the negation.' And the branches built upon this rule include some in which some scholars prefer the establishment of the ruling, based on the view that this type of defect is not invalidating; and some in which they prefer the lapse of the ruling due to the absence of its wisdom, based on the view that this type of defect is invalidating. Among the branches built upon that is the istibra' of a young girl; because the renewal of ownership of a slave woman is the cause for her istibra', and the wisdom of istibra' is verifying the freedom of the womb from pregnancy, and it is already realized in the young girl without istibra'. And like one whose residence is by the sea and he descends from it in a ship that covers the distance of shortening in a moment: is it permissible for him to shorten the prayer and break the fast in Ramadan due to the existence of its cause, which is travel, or is it not permissible for him due to the absence of the wisdom, which is lifting hardship, since there is no hardship on the mentioned person at all? And from them is the legislation of istinja' from the passing of a stone from him without any moisture with it, and the obligation of ghusl on a woman in postpartum bleeding from delivering a dry child with no blood. And from them is that wudu' is not invalidated by touching the penis if no pleasure is felt in the touching with the interior of the palm or fingers. And from them is that wudu' is invalidated by a kiss on the mouth if no pleasure is felt. And from them is that if a man says to his wife: 'You are divorced with the last part of menstruation,' then it is a divorce coinciding with menstruation, and it is a cause for the prohibition of divorce, but it entails the waiting period, so there is no prolongation in it, so the wisdom that necessitates the prohibition, which is prolongation, is absent here. And from them is the case where he says: 'You are divorced with the last part of purity,' then it is the opposite of the previous case. And according to the view of whoever said that the absence of wisdom from the ruling is a defect in the cause, then it is not permissible [at his view] to shorten the prayer and break the fast in Ramadan for one whom hardship does not befall.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition of a key dispute in Islamic legal theory concerning the relationship between a cause ('illa) and its underlying wisdom (hikma). The author begins by defining 'illa and hikma: the 'illa is the attribute (wasf) that is the cause of a ruling (hukm), and the hikma is the purpose or benefit that makes that attribute suitable to be the cause. For example, intoxication is the 'illa for the prohibition of intoxicants, and the preservation of the intellect is the hikma. The author then presents two scholarly positions regarding a scenario where the ruling exists but its wisdom is absent. Some scholars consider this absence (takhalluf) a defect (qaadih) that invalidates the 'illa, because the 'illa was chosen only for its wisdom. Others argue it is not a defect, because when the 'illa is merely an indicator (mazannah) of the wisdom, the ruling is attached to the indicator itself, not directly to the wisdom. The author references the versified work Muraqi as-Su'ud for this dispute. To illustrate the practical implications, the author lists several legal branches (furu') derived from these positions. For instance, the istibra' (waiting period for a new slave woman) is required to ensure the womb is free from pregnancy; for a young girl, this wisdom is already present, so on one view the ruling may not be needed. Another example: a traveler whose sea voyage is so short that he experiences no hardship—can he shorten his prayers? The answer depends on whether the 'illa of travel is considered independently of the hikma of hardship. Further examples include istinja' from a dry stone, ghusl after a dry birth, wudu' invalidated by touching or kissing without pleasure, and divorce pronounced at the end of menstruation or purity. The author concludes by noting that on the view that absence of hikma invalidates the 'illa, the traveler without hardship cannot shorten prayers.

Key Terms
العلة
الحكمة
الحكم
القادح
المظنة

Key Takeaway
The presence or absence of hikma is a central factor in determining the validity of an 'illa, and scholars differ on whether its absence invalidates the 'illa, with practical consequences for fiqh rulings.

The Effect of Absence of Wisdom on the Validity of a Cause
Author presents fiqhi examples illustrating disagreement over absence of hikma as defect in 'illa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the fiqhi disagreement over whether absence of wisdom (hikma) invalidates the cause ('illa), and apply the distinction between rulings attached to wisdom vs. its likelihood (mazanna) to concrete cases.

Core Idea
Absence of wisdom does not always break the cause; the ruling may stand if tied to the likelihood of wisdom.

In Islamic legal theory, the relationship between a cause ('illa) and its underlying wisdom (hikma) is a central point of debate. This passage presents a series of practical fiqhi examples that test whether the ruling must fail when the wisdom is absent. By working through these cases, you will learn to distinguish two major positions and a subtle refinement involving probability (mazanna).

Guided Path
1
Identify the 'illa and hikma in each example
For each fiqhi case (e.g., istibra of young girl, travel by ship, etc.), pinpoint the cause that triggers the ruling and the wisdom that justifies it. Note instances where the cause exists but the wisdom is absent.
For example, in istibra of a young girl: the cause is new ownership, the wisdom is ensuring an empty womb, but the wisdom is already realized without istibra.
2
Determine the ruling's dependency
Decide whether the ruling is attached primarily to the cause itself or to the presence of wisdom. If the wisdom is absent, does the ruling still apply? Record your initial judgment.
3
Compare the two positions
The passage presents two opposing views: one holds that absence of hikma is a defect (qadih) voiding the 'illa, so the ruling is negated. The other holds it is not a defect, so the ruling remains. For each example, state what each position would rule.
E.g., for instant travel: the first says no qasr, the second says qasr is allowed.
4
Examine the mazanna refinement
The second position can be understood in two ways: either the scholar denies the defect entirely, or he accepts the defect but argues the ruling is attached to the likelihood (mazanna) of wisdom, not wisdom itself. This allows the ruling to persist despite actual absence of wisdom.
Mazanna means the ruling is tied to a context where wisdom is typically present, not to its realization.
5
Apply to new cases
Given a novel fiqhi scenario where the cause exists but wisdom is absent, determine which position each ruling implies and justify using the principles from the passage.
Practice with the examples provided: dry delivery, touching without pleasure, etc.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الفروع المبنية على ذلك استبراء الصغيرة؛ لأن تجدّد ملك الأمة علة لاستبرائها، وحكمة الاستبراء هي تحقيق براءة الرحم من الحمل، وهي متحققة في الصغيرة بدون الاستبراء. وكمن مسكنه على البحر ونزل منه في سفينة قطعت به مسافة القصر في لحظة فهل يجوز له قصر الصلاة والفطر في رمضان لوجود علة ذلك وهي السفر، أو لا يجوز له ذلك نظرًا إلى تخلف الحكمة التي هي رفع المشقة إذ لا مشقة على المذكور أصلاً؟ ومنها شرع الاستنجاء من خروج حصاة منه لا بلل معها، ووجوب الغسل على النفساء من وضع الولد جافًا لا دم معه. ومنها عدم نقض الوضوء بمس الذكر إذا لم توجد اللذة في اللمس بباطن الكف أو الأصابع. ومنها نقض الوضوء بالقبلة على الفم إذا لم توجد اللذة. ومنها ما لو قال لامرأته: أنت طالق مع آخر جزء من الحيض؛ فإنه طلاق صادف الحيض، وهو علة لتحريم الطلاق، ولكن يستعقب العدة فلا تطويل فيه، فالحكمة الموجبة للمنع التي هي التطويل منتفية هنا. ومنها ما لو قال: أنت طالق مع آخر جزء من الطهر؛ فإنه عكس الصورة المذكورة قبله. فعلى قول من قال إن تخلف الحكمة عن الحكم قادح في العلة فلا يجوز عنده قصر الصلاة والإفطار في رمضان لمن لم تلحقه مشقة أصلاً، ولا يجب عنده الغسل بخروج الولد جافًا من الدم، ولا ينقض عنده الوضوء بدون اللذة في لمس ذكر أو تقبيل، ولا يمنع عنده الطلاق مع آخر جزء من الحيض لأنه تطويل وإن كان نفس الطلاق واقعا في آخر جزء من الطهر. ومن قال إن هذا النوع من الكسر غير قادح فإنه يلزم على قوله استبراء الصغيرة وجواز القصر والإفطار لمن لم تلحقه مشقة بسفره، والوضوء من حصاة لا بلل معها، ونقض الوضوء باللمس وإن لم توجد اللذة، إلى آخر ما ذكرنا من المسائل المبنية على الخلاف في هذه المسألة. والحاصل أن من قال من أهل العلم: إن الحكم منتف في المسائل المذكورة لانتفاء علته فهو قول منه بأن هذا النوع من الكسر قادح، وعلى قوله فلا إشكال؛ لأن العلة إذا بطلت بالكسر المذكور لم يوجد حكمها لعدم وجود علته. ومن قال من أهل العلم: إن الحكم فيها باقٍ مع تخلف الحكمة فلقوله توجيهان: أحدهما: أنه قائل بأن هذا النوع من الكسر ليس بقادح. والثاني: أنه قادح ويجيب عن عدم القدح به في المسائل المذكورة بأنها إنما عُلق الحكم بها بمظنة وجود الحكمة، والمعروف أن المعلل بالمظان لا يتخلف الحكم فيه بتخلف الحكمة؛ لأن الحكم فيه منوط بالمظنة لا بنفس الحكمة. وأشار بعض أهل العلم إلى هذا.
Among the branches built upon that is the istibra of a young girl, because the renewal of ownership of a slave woman is the cause for istibra, and the wisdom of istibra is to ascertain the purity of the womb from pregnancy, and that is realized in the young girl without istibra. Also, one whose home is on the sea and he descends from it in a ship that carries him the distance of shortening in a moment: is it permissible for him to shorten the prayer and break the fast in Ramadan due to the existence of its cause, which is travel, or is it not permissible for him looking at the absence of wisdom, which is the removal of hardship, since there is no hardship at all for the mentioned? Among them is the legislation of istinja from the exit of a pebble from him with no moisture accompanying it, and the obligation of ghusl on a woman in postpartum from delivering a child dry with no blood with it. Among them is the non-breaking of wudu by touching the penis if no pleasure exists in the touch with the palm or fingers. Among them is the breaking of wudu by kissing on the mouth if no pleasure exists. Among them is if he said to his wife: 'You are divorced with the last part of menstruation,' then it is a divorce coinciding with menstruation, and it is a cause for prohibition of divorce, but it entails the waiting period so there is no prolongation, so the wisdom necessitating the prohibition, which is prolongation, is absent here. Among them is if he said: 'You are divorced with the last part of purity,' then it is the reverse of the image mentioned before it. So according to the one who said that the absence of wisdom from the ruling is a defect in the cause, then he does not permit shortening the prayer and breaking the fast in Ramadan for one whom no hardship at all occurred, and he does not deem obligatory ghusl for the exit of a dry child from blood, and he does not break wudu without pleasure in touching a penis or kissing, and he does not prohibit divorce with the last part of menstruation because it is prolongation, even if the divorce itself occurs in the last part of purity. And whoever said that this type of defect is not a defect, then his statement necessitates istibra of a young girl, and permissibility of shortening and breaking fast for one whom no hardship occurred in his travel, and wudu from a pebble with no moisture, and breaking wudu by touching even if no pleasure exists, to the end of the issues we mentioned built upon the disagreement in this question. And the conclusion is that whoever among the people of knowledge said that the ruling is negated in the mentioned issues due to the negation of its cause, it is a statement from him that this type of defect is a defect, and according to his statement there is no problem, because if the cause is invalidated by the mentioned defect, its ruling does not exist, due to the non-existence of its cause. And whoever among the people of knowledge said that the ruling remains in them despite the absence of wisdom, then his statement has two interpretations: one: that he says this type of defect is not a defect. The second: that it is a defect and he answers about the non-defect by it in the mentioned issues by saying that the ruling is attached to the likelihood of the existence of wisdom, and it is known that what is justified by likelihoods does not have its ruling absent due to the absence of wisdom, because the ruling is dependent on the likelihood, not on wisdom itself. And some of the people of knowledge alluded to this.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage expands on the dialectical principle regarding the relationship between 'illa (cause) and hikma (wisdom) by presenting a series of fiqhi examples. The author first enumerates several cases where the cause for a ruling exists, but the underlying wisdom is absent: istibra of a young girl (cause: new ownership; wisdom: ensuring womb is empty, but realized anyway), travel by ship in an instant (cause: travel; wisdom: hardship, but no hardship), istinja from a dry pebble, ghusl for a dry delivery, wudu not broken by touching without pleasure, wudu broken by kissing without pleasure, and divorce at the end of menstruation (cause: menstruation prohibits divorce; wisdom: prolongation, but divorce at that time still has waiting period, so no prolongation in effect). The author then presents two opposing positions. The first position holds that absence of hikma is a defect (qadih) in the 'illa, thus the ruling is negated when hikma is absent. For example, one who holds this would say travel without hardship does not permit shortening prayer. The second position holds that this type of defect is not a defect, so the ruling remains despite absence of hikma; this leads to rulings like istibra of the young girl and qasr for the instant traveler. The author then refines: those who say the ruling remains with absent hikma have two interpretations. One: they truly believe absence of hikma is not a defect. Two: they accept it as a defect but argue that the ruling is attached to the likelihood (mazanna) of the hikma, not the hikma itself, so the hikma's absence does not remove the ruling. The author notes that some scholars alluded to this second interpretation. The passage is a clear example of jadal (dialectical) reasoning in usul al-fiqh, demonstrating how theoretical principles have concrete ramifications.

Key Terms
علة ('illa): The effective cause that grounds a legal ruling; the text discusses whether its validity depends on the actual presence of its underlying wisdom. [Note: In mantiq, 'illa is a broader term for any cause; here it is specifically the legal cause in fiqh (hukm shar'i).]
حكمة (hikma): The wisdom, purpose, or benefit behind a legal ruling; its absence may or may not affect the ruling's validity according to different scholars.
كسر (kasar): A defect or break in the causal link; the text uses 'هذا النوع من الكسر' to refer specifically to the absence of hikma while 'illa exists.
مظنة (mazanna): A likely indicator or place of expectation; rulings attached to a mazanna do not fail when the expected wisdom is absent. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, mazanna often refers to a circumstantial indicator of a rule's presence.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a necessary consequence of holding that absence of hikma is a defect (qadih) in the 'illa?
A) Shortening prayer is not permitted for one who experiences no hardship in travel.
B) The young girl requires istibra despite having no possibility of pregnancy.
C) Wudu is not broken by touching a penis without pleasure.
D) Divorce at the end of menstruation is prohibited because it entails prolongation.

Key Takeaway
Whether the absence of wisdom invalidates the cause depends on whether the ruling is attached to the wisdom itself or to its likelihood (mazanna).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For each of the following fiqhi cases, identify whether the ruling would be upheld or negated according to the first position (qadih) and according to the second position (non-qadih): (a) istibra of a young girl, (b) qasr for a traveler without hardship, (c) ghusl for a dry delivery.
?
Construct your own fiqhi example where the 'illa exists but the hikma is absent, then state the ruling under each of the two positions, and explain which interpretation of the second position (denial of defect or mazanna attachment) would apply.
