The Effect of a Broken Cause (Kasar) on Legal Rulings
Author discusses the implications of accepting or rejecting the validity of a broken cause in legal reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main positions regarding the effect of a broken cause in Usul al-Fiqh and understand their implications on branched rulings.

Core Idea
The presence or absence of a break in the cause determines the validity of the legal ruling.

In Usul al-Fiqh, the concept of 'kasar' (break) in the 'illa (effective cause) is critical. This passage explores the consequences of accepting or rejecting the validity of a broken cause, and how it affects several subsidiary rulings. Understanding this debate allows you to evaluate how jurists derive rulings based on causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What is the role of the cause ('illa) in legal rulings? The passage discusses whether a break in the cause invalidates the ruling.
2
List the two scholarly positions
One position holds the break is qadih (defect) and negates the ruling; the other holds the ruling remains despite the break.
3
Examine the first position's implications
If the break is qadih, then rulings like ghusl after dry birth, wudu after touch without pleasure, etc., are negated because the cause is absent.
4
Examine the second position's two interpretations
The second position has two interpretations: either the break is not a defect, or it is a defect but the ruling is attached to a presumption (mazanna) of the wisdom, not the wisdom itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مشقة أصلاً، ولا يجب عنده الغُسْل بخروج الولد جافًا من الدم، ولا ينقض عنده الوضوء بدون اللذة في لمس ذكر أو تقبيل، ولا يُمنع عنده الطلاق مع آخر جزء من الحيض لأنه تطويل وإن كان نفس الطلاق واقعا في آخر جزء من الطهر .
ومن قال إن هذا النوع من الكسر غير قادح) فإنه يلزم على قوله استبراء الصغيرة وجواز القصر والإفطار لمن لم تلحقه مشقة بسفره، والوضوء من حصاة لا بلل معها، ونقض الوضوء باللمس وإن لم توجد اللذة، إلى آخر ما ذكرنا من المسائل المبنية على الخلاف في هذه المسألة .
والحاصل أن من قال من أهل العلم: إن الحكم منتف في المسائل المذكورة لانتفاء علته فهو قول منه بأن هذا النوع من الكسر قادح، وعلى قوله فلا إشكال ؛ لأن العلة إذا بطلت بالكسر المذكور لم يوجد حكمها ؛ لعدم وجود علته .
ومن قال من أهل العلم : إن الحكم فيها باقٍ مع تخلف الحكمة فلقوله توجیهان :
أحدهما : أنه قائل بأن هذا النوع من الكسر ليس بقادح .
والثاني: أنه قادح ويجيب عن عدم القدح به في المسائل المذكورة بأنها إنما عُلق الحكم بها بمظنّة وجود الحكمة، والمعروف أن المعلل بالمظان لا يتخلف الحكم فيه بتخلف الحكمة؛ لأن الحكم فيه منوط بالمظنة لا بنفس الحكمة ؛ وأشار بعض أهل العلم إلى هذا بقوله :
إن عُلّل الحكم بعلةٍ غَلَبْ وجودها اكتفي بذا عن الطلب لها بكل صورة إلخ .
وعلى هذا فالمانع من القدح بهذا النوع من الكسر إناطة الحكم بمظنة الحكمة لا بنفس الحكمة؛ وذلك لأن نفس الحكمة ربما لا يمكن انضباطها في بعض الأحوال .
كما لو عُلّق حكم قصر الصلاة وجواز الإفطار في رمضان مثلاً بحصول المشقة، فإن هذه الحكمة لا تنضبط؛ لاختلافها باختلاف الأشخاص والأحوال والأزمنة، فأنيط الحكم بسفر أربعة برد مثلاً ؛ لأنه مظنة المشقة، ومن هنا لم يُنظر إلا للمظنة، وألغي نفس الحكمة عند من يقول بذلك، فأجاز الفطر والقصر لمن سافر أربعة برد وإن لم تلحقه مشقة؛ لأن سفرها مظنة المشقة، وهو حاصل، فاكتفي بمظنة الحكمة عن نفس الحكمة .
واعلم أن هذه المسائل التي ذكرنا أنها مفرعة على هذه المسألة مختلفٌ فيها بين أهل العلم، فمن أثبت الحكم مع تخلف الحكمة فهو إما قائل بأن ذلك الكسر غير قادح، أو أنه قادح منع من اعتباره إناطة الحكم بالمظنة .
Hardship essentially, and according to him bath is not obligatory when the child comes out dry of blood, and ablution is not invalidated without pleasure in touching the genitals or kissing, and divorce is not prevented at the last part of menstruation because it is prolongation, even if the divorce itself occurs at the last part of purity. And whoever says that this type of break is not a defect, then his statement necessitates [accepting] the istibra' of a young girl and the permissibility of shortening [prayer] and breaking fast for one who does not experience hardship in his travel, and ablution from a pebble without moisture, and invalidation of ablution by touch even if pleasure is not found, up to the issues we mentioned based on the disagreement in this matter. The conclusion is that whoever among the scholars says that the ruling is negated in the mentioned issues due to the absence of its cause, then this is his statement that this type of break is a defect, and according to his statement there is no problem; because if the cause is invalidated by the mentioned break, its ruling does not exist due to the non-existence of its cause. And whoever among the scholars says that the ruling remains in them despite the absence of the wisdom, then his statement has two interpretations: One of them: that he says that this type of break is not a defect. And the second: that it is a defect, and he answers the non-defectiveness in the mentioned issues by saying that the ruling was attached to them by a presumption of the existence of the wisdom, and it is known that what is attached to presumptions does not have its ruling altered by the absence of the wisdom; because the ruling therein depends on the presumption, not on the wisdom itself. And some scholars indicated this by his saying: 'If the ruling is attached to a cause whose existence is predominant, that suffices in seeking it in every case, etc.' On this basis, the preventer of defectiveness by this type of break is the attachment of the ruling to the presumption of wisdom, not to the wisdom itself; because the wisdom itself sometimes cannot be regulated in some situations. As if the ruling of shortening prayer and permissibility of breaking fast in Ramadan, for example, were attached to the occurrence of hardship, then this wisdom cannot be regulated due to its varying with different persons, circumstances, and times, so the ruling was attached to traveling four barid, for example, because it is a presumption of hardship. Hence, only the presumption is considered, and the wisdom itself is ignored according to whoever says that. So he permits breaking fast and shortening prayer for one who traveled four barid even if hardship does not befall him, because traveling that distance is a presumption of hardship, and it is present, so the presumption of wisdom suffices over the wisdom itself. And know that these issues that we mentioned as branching off from this matter are disagreed upon among scholars. So whoever affirms the ruling despite the absence of wisdom, then he is either saying that break is not a defect, or that it is a defect but he prevents its consideration by the attachment of the ruling to the presumption.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical discussion in Usul al-Fiqh about the effect of a 'break' (kasar) in the effective cause ('illa) on the legal ruling. The author begins by listing several furu' (branched) issues that arise from the disagreement, such as whether ghusl is obligatory when a child is born dry, or whether wudu is invalidated by touching without pleasure. He then states the core issue: whether a break in the cause (i.e., a missing condition) invalidates the ruling. Two scholarly positions are outlined. The first position holds that if the cause is broken, the ruling is negated because the cause is absent. The second position maintains that the ruling remains even when the wisdom (hikma) behind the cause is absent. For this second position, there are two interpretations: either the break is not considered a defect at all, or it is a defect but the ruling is attached to a presumption (mazanna) of the wisdom, not to the wisdom itself. The author explains the concept of ta'lil bi al-mazanna (causal reasoning based on presumption), giving the example of travel of four barid as a presumption of hardship, which justifies the permissibility of shortening prayer and breaking fast even if no actual hardship occurs. The passage concludes by reiterating that all these furu' issues are subject to disagreement based on the foundational stance on this mas'ala.

Key Terms
الكسر
العلة
الحكمة
المظنة
قدح

Checkpoint
According to the text, what are the two interpretations for those who affirm the ruling despite the absence of wisdom?
A) The break is not a defect, or it is a defect but the ruling is attached to a presumption.
B) The break is never a defect, or the wisdom is always present.
C) The ruling is always negated, or it is affirmed by consensus.
D) The cause is invalid, or the ruling is based on public interest.

Key Takeaway
The effect of a break in the cause on the ruling depends on whether the break is considered a defect and whether the ruling is attached to a presumption of wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a ruling from your own fiqh study that depends on a particular cause. Explain whether a break in that cause would change the ruling according to the two positions.
?
For the example of traveling four barid, explain why the wisdom (hardship) can be absent yet the ruling (shortening prayer) still applies, according to the second interpretation.

Presumption (Mazanna) as a Substitute for Wisdom
Author discusses the implications of accepting or rejecting the validity of a broken cause in legal reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of 'ta'lil bi al-mazanna' (causal reasoning based on presumption) and apply it to legal examples.

Core Idea
When wisdom cannot be pinpointed, presumption (mazanna) steps in to anchor the ruling.

The passage introduces a subtle tool: when the wisdom behind a cause is variable and non-regularable, jurists attach the ruling to a presumption of that wisdom. This lesson unpacks the example of travel as a presumption for hardship, showing how a ruling can apply even when actual hardship is absent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Why can't wisdom (hikma) always be used as the direct cause? The text explains that wisdom varies with persons, circumstances, and times, making it non-regularable.
2
Define mazanna
A presumption (mazanna) is a likely indication that the wisdom is present. It serves as a substitute when the wisdom itself is difficult to ascertain.
3
Analyze the travel example
Traveling four barid is a mazanna for hardship. The ruling of shortening prayer is attached to this presumption, not to actual hardship.
4
Apply the concept
How does this resolve the apparent conflict when the break occurs but the ruling remains? The break is considered a defect, but because the ruling is attached to the mazanna, it does not require the actual wisdom to be present.

Source Text

Hardship essentially, and according to him bath is not obligatory when the child comes out dry of blood, and ablution is not invalidated without pleasure in touching the genitals or kissing, and divorce is not prevented at the last part of menstruation because it is prolongation, even if the divorce itself occurs at the last part of purity. And whoever says that this type of break is not a defect, then his statement necessitates [accepting] the istibra' of a young girl and the permissibility of shortening [prayer] and breaking fast for one who does not experience hardship in his travel, and ablution from a pebble without moisture, and invalidation of ablution by touch even if pleasure is not found, up to the issues we mentioned based on the disagreement in this matter. The conclusion is that whoever among the scholars says that the ruling is negated in the mentioned issues due to the absence of its cause, then this is his statement that this type of break is a defect, and according to his statement there is no problem; because if the cause is invalidated by the mentioned break, its ruling does not exist due to the non-existence of its cause. And whoever among the scholars says that the ruling remains in them despite the absence of the wisdom, then his statement has two interpretations: One of them: that he says that this type of break is not a defect. And the second: that it is a defect, and he answers the non-defectiveness in the mentioned issues by saying that the ruling was attached to them by a presumption of the existence of the wisdom, and it is known that what is attached to presumptions does not have its ruling altered by the absence of the wisdom; because the ruling therein depends on the presumption, not on the wisdom itself. And some scholars indicated this by his saying: 'If the ruling is attached to a cause whose existence is predominant, that suffices in seeking it in every case, etc.' On this basis, the preventer of defectiveness by this type of break is the attachment of the ruling to the presumption of wisdom, not to the wisdom itself; because the wisdom itself sometimes cannot be regulated in some situations. As if the ruling of shortening prayer and permissibility of breaking fast in Ramadan, for example, were attached to the occurrence of hardship, then this wisdom cannot be regulated due to its varying with different persons, circumstances, and times, so the ruling was attached to traveling four barid, for example, because it is a presumption of hardship. Hence, only the presumption is considered, and the wisdom itself is ignored according to whoever says that. So he permits breaking fast and shortening prayer for one who traveled four barid even if hardship does not befall him, because traveling that distance is a presumption of hardship, and it is present, so the presumption of wisdom suffices over the wisdom itself. And know that these issues that we mentioned as branching off from this matter are disagreed upon among scholars. So whoever affirms the ruling despite the absence of wisdom, then he is either saying that break is not a defect, or that it is a defect but he prevents its consideration by the attachment of the ruling to the presumption.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical discussion in Usul al-Fiqh about the effect of a 'break' (kasar) in the effective cause ('illa) on the legal ruling. The author begins by listing several furu' (branched) issues that arise from the disagreement, such as whether ghusl is obligatory when a child is born dry, or whether wudu is invalidated by touching without pleasure. He then states the core issue: whether a break in the cause (i.e., a missing condition) invalidates the ruling. Two scholarly positions are outlined. The first position holds that if the cause is broken, the ruling is negated because the cause is absent. The second position maintains that the ruling remains even when the wisdom (hikma) behind the cause is absent. For this second position, there are two interpretations: either the break is not considered a defect at all, or it is a defect but the ruling is attached to a presumption (mazanna) of the wisdom, not to the wisdom itself. The author explains the concept of ta'lil bi al-mazanna (causal reasoning based on presumption), giving the example of travel of four barid as a presumption of hardship, which justifies the permissibility of shortening prayer and breaking fast even if no actual hardship occurs. The passage concludes by reiterating that all these furu' issues are subject to disagreement based on the foundational stance on this mas'ala.

الحكمة
العلة
الكسر
قدح

D) Because the Prophet specified that distance.

Key Takeaway
Presumption (mazanna) is a key tool in Usul al-Fiqh for attaching rulings when the underlying wisdom is not reliably regulable.

Defending an Analogy from Kasar Using Mazannah
Dialectical defense against a kasar using mazannah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a kasar of the 'ruling absent despite wisdom present' type and evaluate its validity using the distinction between 'illah (cause) and mazannah (probable cause).

Core Idea
A kasar on wisdom fails when the ruling is on a cause, not the wisdom.

In usul al-fiqh debates, opponents often try to break an analogy by showing that the wisdom (hikmah) exists but the ruling does not. This type of kasar can be deflected by showing that the ruling is attached to a probable cause (mazannah), not to the wisdom itself. The Hanafi defense of travel concessions illustrates this move: travel is the cause, not hardship. Let's unpack this dialectical strategy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target analogy
Locate the Hanafi claim: the sinful traveler is a traveler, so he gets concessions like any other traveler. The cause ('illah) is travel, and the ruling is dispensation.
Travel is the mazannah of hardship, but the ruling is linked to travel, not hardship.
2
Pinpoint the kasar objection
Find the objection: strenuous work in the settled area (like carrying heavy loads) also involves hardship, yet no dispensation is granted. The opponent says: this shows the analogy fails because wisdom exists but ruling does not.
This is a kasar by تخلف الحكم عن الحكمة (absence of ruling despite presence of wisdom).
3
Distinguish cause from wisdom
The Hanafi answer: the cause is travel, not hardship. The wisdom (hardship) may be absent in some travels or present in non-travels, but the cause remains travel. The objection only finds wisdom, not the cause.
The cause ('illah) must be present for the ruling to apply; here it is not.
4
Evaluate the kasar's validity
Since the cause is absent in the objection, the kasar does not strike at the cause. Hence it is not a valid refutation. The ruling is attached to a mazannah, not to every instance of wisdom.
The author states this type of kasar is 'غير قادح' (not detrimental).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقوله : إن عُلّل الحكم بعلةٍ غَلَبْ وجودها اكتفي بذا عن الطلب لها بكل صورة إلخ . وعليهذا فالمانع من القدح بهذا النوع من الكسر إناطة الحكم بمظنة الحكمة لا بنفس الحكمة؛ وذلك لأن نفس الحكمة ربما لا يمكن انضباطها في بعض الأحوال . كما لو عُلّق حكم قصر الصلاة وجواز الإفطار في رمضان مثلاً بحصول المشقة، فإن هذه الحكمة لا تنضبط؛ لاختلافها باختلاف وو الأشخاص والأحوال والأزمنة، فأنيط الحكم بسفر أربعة برد (۱) مثلاً ؛ لأنه مظنة المشقة ومن هنا لم ينظر إلا للمظنة، هنا لم ينظر إلا للمظنة، وألغي نفس الحكمة عند من يقول بذلك، فأجاز الفطر والقصر لمن سافر أربعة برد وإن لم تلحقه مشقة؛ لأن سفرها مظنة المشقة، وهو حاصل، فاكتفي بمظنة الحكمة عن نفس الحكمة . واعلم أن هذه المسائل التي ذكرنا أنها مفرعة على هذه المسألة مختلف فيها بين أهل العلم، فمن أثبت الحكم مع تخلف الحكمة فهو إما قائل بأن ذلك الكسر غير قادح، أو أنه قادح منع من اعتباره إناطة الحكم بالمظنة . (۱) ومن نفي فيها الحكم لتخلف حكمته فهو قائل بأن الكسر المذكور جمع بريد والبريد يساوي اثني عشر ميلاً . الناظر وأما تخلف الحكم عن حكمته فهو أحد أنواع الكسر ، والأظهر أن (1) هذا النوع من الكسر غير قادح وجزم بذلك ابن قدامة في روضة واختاره ابن الحاجب في بعض المواضع في مختصره الأصولي(٢)، ومثل له بقول الحنفي في المسافر العاصي بسفره : مسافر، فيترخص بسفره كغير العاصي، فإذا قيل له : ولم قلت إن السفر علة للترخيص ؟ قال : بالمناسبة لما فيه من المشقة المقتضية للترخيص ؛ لأنه تخفيف، وهو نفع للمترخص . فيعترض عليه بصنعة شاقة في الحضر، كحمل الأثقال وضرب المعاول وما يوجب قرب النار في ظهيرة القيظ في القطر الحار، فههنا قد وجدت الحكمة وهي المشقة، ولم يوجد الحكم الذي هو قصر الصلاة وإباحة الفطر مثلاً . والجواب عن هذا الكسر بتخلف الحكم مع وجود الحكمة أن الشرع إنما اعتبر مشقة السفر، فالعلة في الترخيص المذكور السفر، وحكمتها رفع المشقة، والتخفيف على المسافر، فأصل العلة لم يوجد في هذا الكسر، وإنما وجدت فيه الحكمة فقط، فلم يقع كسر في العلة ؛ لعدم وجودها أصلاً في صورة الكسر المذكور، وسفر العاصي بسفره علة للترخيص، والمانع من تأثيرها عند من يقول بذلك أن (1) ص • (٢) (٤٧/٣) مع شرح الأصفهاني، جامعة أم القرى، ١٤٠٦هـ.
By his saying: 'If the ruling is ascribed to a cause whose existence predominates, one suffices with that over seeking it in every case, etc.' Based on this, the prevention of objection by this type of kasar is the attachment of the ruling to the probable cause of the wisdom, not to the wisdom itself. That is because the wisdom itself perhaps cannot be controlled in some situations. As if the ruling of shortening prayer and permitting breaking fast in Ramadan, for example, were attached to the occurrence of hardship, then this wisdom cannot be controlled due to its variation with the variation of persons, circumstances, and times. So the ruling was attached to traveling four barids (1), for example, because it is a probable cause of hardship. Hence only the probable cause is considered—here only the probable cause is considered—and the wisdom itself is nullified according to those who say that. So they permitted breaking fast and shortening prayer for one who travels four barids even if no hardship befalls him, because his travel is a probable cause of hardship, and it is present. Thus one suffices with the probable cause of the wisdom over the wisdom itself. Know that these issues we mentioned as derived from this matter are disputed among scholars. Whoever affirms the ruling despite the omission of the wisdom is either saying that such kasar is not detrimental, or that it is detrimental but prevented by the attachment of the ruling to the probable cause. Ibn Qudamah affirmed this in Rawdah, and Ibn al-Hajib chose it in some places in his Mukhtasar al-Usuli. He gave as an example the saying of the Hanafi regarding the sinful traveler: he is a traveler, so he may avail of dispensations in his travel like the non-sinful traveler. If it is said to him: Why do you say that travel is the cause for dispensation? He says: By suitability due to the hardship it contains that necessitates dispensation, because it is a lightening, and it is a benefit for the one granted dispensation. Then it is objected against him by a strenuous occupation in the settled area, such as carrying heavy loads, using pickaxes, and what necessitates proximity to fire at noon in the heat in a hot region. Here the wisdom, namely hardship, is found, but the ruling, which is shortening prayer and permitting breaking fast, for example, is not found. The answer to this kasar of the omission of the ruling with the presence of the wisdom is that the Law only considered the hardship of travel. So the cause for the mentioned dispensation is travel, and its wisdom is the removal of hardship and lightening for the traveler. Therefore the root cause is not found in this kasar; only the wisdom is found. So no kasar occurs in the cause due to its non-existence at all in the mentioned image of the kasar. The travel of the sinful traveler is a cause for dispensation, and the preventive from its effectiveness according to those who say that is that (1). Commentary
This passage is a dialectical commentary on the application of 'kasar' (inconsistency/objection) in legal reasoning, specifically when a ruling is attached to a 'mazannah' (probable cause) rather than the 'hikmah' (underlying wisdom). The author begins by citing a previous statement that if a ruling is based on a cause that predominantly exists, one need not look for it in every instance. He then explains that this prevents objection by a certain type of 'kasar' because the ruling is attached to the probable cause, not the wisdom itself, since wisdom may vary and be unregulable. An example is given: fasting and prayer concessions in Ramadan are attached to travel distance (four barids) as a probable cause of hardship, so even if no hardship occurs, the concession applies. The author notes that scholars differ on the derived issues: some say the 'kasar' is not detrimental; others say it is but is prevented by the attachment to the probable cause. Then, focusing on the 'kasar' where the ruling is absent despite the wisdom being present, the author states that the dominant view is that this type is not detrimental, citing Ibn Qudamah and Ibn al-Hajib. The Hanafi argument for the sinful traveler is used as an example: travel is a cause for dispensation due to suitability (hardship). An objection is raised: strenuous work in the settled area involves hardship but no dispensation, so why is it different? The answer distinguishes between the cause (travel) and the wisdom (hardship). The cause itself is absent in the objection; only the wisdom is present. Thus, the 'kasar' does not invalidate the cause because the cause is not there. The passage concludes by noting that the sinful traveler's travel is a cause, but there is a preventive factor according to some (the text cuts off).

The Non-Damaging Objection of Absence of Ruling Despite Wisdom
Author explains a non-damaging type of kasar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the absence of a ruling due to a missing cause versus missing wisdom, and evaluate whether such an objection is damaging in jadal.

Core Idea
An objection that the ruling is absent despite wisdom is not damaging if the cause itself is absent.

In dialectical reasoning (jadal), a common objection is to point out that the wisdom (hikmah) underlying a cause ('illa) is present but the ruling is not. This seems to show inconsistency. However, the author argues this type of objection (kasar) is not damaging because the cause itself is absent. We will examine the reasoning and apply it to a fiqh example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components: cause, wisdom, and ruling
In the example, travel is the cause ('illa), hardship is the wisdom (hikmah), and the concession (e.g., shortening prayer) is the ruling (hukm). Distinguish these three in the argument.
The cause is what the Law directly considers; the wisdom is the benefit intended.
2
Understand the objection
The objector points to tasks in settled areas that involve hardship (wisdom) but lack the concession (ruling). This seems to challenge the link between wisdom and ruling.
The objector assumes that if hardship justifies concession, then any hardship should warrant concession.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author argues that the cause is travel, not hardship. In the settled-area tasks, travel is absent, so the cause is missing. The objection only proves that wisdom alone is insufficient; it does not invalidate the cause.
The absence of the cause means the objection does not target the original inference.
4
Apply to a similar scenario
Given a new objection, check whether the cause is present or only the wisdom. If the cause is absent, the objection is non-damaging. For example, if someone objects that a sick person does not get travel concessions even though they have hardship, the cause (travel) is missing, so the objection fails.
This principle protects valid analogies from superficial challenges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تخلف الحكم عن حكمته فهو أحد أنواع الكسر، والأظهر أن هذا النوع من الكسر غير قادح وجزم بذلك ابن قدامة في روضة واختاره ابن الحاجب في بعض المواضع في مختصره الأصولي، ومثل له بقول الحنفي في المسافر العاصي بسفره : مسافر، فيترخص بسفره كغير العاصي، فإذا قيل له : ولم قلت إن السفر علة للترخيص ؟ قال : بالمناسبة لما فيه من المشقة المقتضية للترخيص ؛ لأنه تخفيف، وهو نفع للمترخّص . فيُعترض عليه بصنعة شاقة في الحضر، كحمل الأثقال وضرب المعاول وما يوجب قرب النار في ظهيرة القيظ في القُطر الحار، فههنا قد وجدت الحكمة وهي المشقة، ولم يوجد الحكم الذي هو قصر الصلاة وإباحة الفطر مثلاً . والجواب عن هذا الكسر بتخلف الحكم مع وجود الحكمة أن الشرع إنما اعتبر مشقة السفر، فالعلة في الترخيص المذكور السفر، وحكمتها رفع المشقة، والتخفيف على المسافر، فأصل العلة لم يوجد في هذا الكسر، وإنما وجدت فيه الحكمة فقط، فلم يقع كسر في العلة ؛ لعدم وجودها أصلاً في صورة الكسر المذكور، وسفر العاصي بسفره علة للترخيص، والمانع من تأثيرها عند من يقول بذلك أن الترخيص تخفيف، والتخفيف على العاصي إعانة له على معصيته، والله - جل وعلا - يقول : ﴿ وَلَا تَعَاوَنُوا عَلَى الْإِثْمِ وَالْعُدْوَانِ وَاتَّقُوا ﴾ [المائدة .[٢
As for the absence of the ruling from its wisdom, it is one of the types of objection (kasar). The most apparent [view] is that this type of objection is not damaging. Ibn Qudamah affirmed this in Rawdah, and Ibn al-Hajib chose it in some places in his Mukhtasar al-Usuli. He gave an example with the statement of the Hanafi regarding the traveler who is disobedient in his travel: 'He is a traveler, so he may avail himself of concessions in his travel like the non-disobedient.' If it is said to him: 'Why did you say that travel is the cause for concession?' He said: 'Because of appropriateness due to the hardship it entails that necessitates concession, because it is a lightening, and it is a benefit for the one who receives the concession.' So it is objected against him by a difficult profession in the settled area, such as carrying heavy loads, striking with picks, and what necessitates proximity to fire at noon in the hot season in the hot region. Here, the wisdom (hardship) is found, but the ruling (shortening prayer and permitting breaking fast, for example) is not found. And the answer to this objection of the absence of the ruling along with the presence of the wisdom is that the Law only considered the hardship of travel, so the cause in the mentioned concession is travel, and its wisdom is removing hardship and lightening upon the traveler. Thus the origin of the cause is not found in this objection; only the wisdom is found. So no objection occurred against the cause, due to its absence entirely in the mentioned form of objection. And the travel of the disobedient traveler is a cause for concession, and the preventer of its effect according to those who say that is that the concession is a lightening, and lightening upon the disobedient is assistance to him in his disobedience. And Allah, may He be glorified and exalted, says: 'And do not cooperate in sin and aggression, and fear Allah.' [Al-Ma'idah 5:2]
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific type of objection (kasar) in dialectical reasoning: the case where the ruling (hukm) is absent despite the presence of the wisdom (hikmah). The author begins by stating that this type of objection is generally considered non-damaging, and he mentions that Ibn Qudamah and Ibn al-Hajib adopted this view. He then provides a detailed example from Hanafi fiqh: a traveler who is disobedient in his travel (e.g., traveling for a sinful purpose) is still considered a traveler and may avail himself of the concessions of travel (such as shortening prayer and breaking fast) just like a non-disobedient traveler. The reasoning is that travel is the cause ('illa) for the concession due to the hardship it entails. An objector might point out that there are hard tasks in the settled area (e.g., carrying heavy loads) that also involve hardship, yet the concession is not granted there, so the presence of hardship (wisdom) without the ruling shows a flaw. The author's response is that the cause ('illa) is specifically travel, not mere hardship. In the objection, the cause (travel) is absent; only the wisdom (hardship) is present. Therefore, the objection does not actually target the cause itself, so it is not damaging. The author then addresses the example of the disobedient traveler, noting that some might argue that granting concession to a sinner is assisting in sin, but that is a different issue (prevention of effect) that does not invalidate the cause. Thus, this type of kasar is not considered a real flaw.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, an objection that the ruling is absent despite wisdom only damages the argument if the cause itself is present; otherwise, it is irrelevant.

Invalidating Part of a Composite Cause and Providing a Substitute
Author defines the type of kasar that invalidates part of a composite cause, and explains the requirement of a substitute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the objection of invalidating part of a composite cause in a dialectical exchange and construct a valid substitute to restore the proof.

Core Idea
A composite cause can survive the invalidation of one part if a valid substitute is provided.

In dialectical reasoning (jadal), one of the most effective objections is to show that a part of the composite cause is irrelevant. However, the arguer is not defenseless: if he can replace the invalidated part with a valid substitute, the objection collapses. This lesson walks through the anatomy of this objection and the strategy of substitution, using a classic example from the obligation of the fear prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the composite cause
Find the two or more descriptions that together form the 'illa (cause). In the example, the cause is 'a prayer that must be made up'. The two parts are 'prayer' and 'must be made up'.
A composite cause always has at least two descriptions.
2
Detect the objector's invalidation
The objector shows that one part is irrelevant (mulgha) by demonstrating that the ruling exists when that part is absent. Here, the objector shows that Hajj, though not a prayer, also has the ruling of obligatory performance and make-up, so 'prayer' is not exclusive.
The test for irrelevance is whether the ruling holds without that description.
3
Determine if a substitute is provided
The arguer must replace the invalidated part with a description that is valid. The substitute must be general enough to cover both the original case and the objector's counterexample. The arguer replaces 'prayer' with 'worship'.
A substitute is only valid if it can serve as a proper part of the 'illa.
4
Validate the substitute
Check that the substitute is not itself irrelevant and that it fits the ruling. 'Worship' is a valid substitute because both prayer and Hajj are acts of worship, and the ruling applies to both.
If no valid substitute exists, the objection stands and the argument fails.
5
Conclude the outcome
Since a valid substitute was provided, the objection is nullified and the original proof is restored. The arguer's argument remains valid.
The success of the substitution depends on the arguer's ability to find a suitable replacement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أنواع الكسر إبطال المعترض جزءا من المعنى المعلَّل به، ونقصُه ما تبقى من أجزاء ذلك المعنى المعلل به فعلم أن هذا النوع من الكسر لا يكون إلا في العلل المركبة من وصفين فأكثر . والقدح بهذا النوع مقيد بعجز المستدل عن الإتيان ببدل من الوصف الذي أبطله المعترض، فإن ذكر بدلاً صالحًا لأن يكون علة الحكم أُلغي الكسر واستقام الدليل . وإبطال الجزء بأن يبيّن المعترض أنه مُلغى بوجود الحكم عند انتفائه، والمراد بنقص الباقي عدم تأثيره في الحكم، وله صورتان : الأولى: أن يأتي المستدلِ ببدل الوصف المسقط عن الاعتبار، كأن يقول في وجوب أداء صلاة الخوف : هي صلاة يجب قضاؤها لو لم تفعل فيجب أداؤها قياسًا على صلاة الأمن؛ فإنها كما يجب قضاؤها لو لم تفعل يجب أداؤها، فوجوب القضاء هو العلة، ووجوب الأداء هو الحكم المعلل بتلك العلة. فيقولُ المعترض : خصوص الصلاة في دليلك مُلغَى، ويبين بأن الحج واجب الأداء كالقضاء . ذلك فيبدل المستدل خصوص الصلاة الذي أبطله المعترض بوصف عام وهو العبادة، فيقول : هي عبادة يجب قضاؤها لو لم تفعل
And among the types of objection (kasar) is the objector's invalidation of part of the meaning that is the cause (al-ma'na al-mu'allal bihi), and the remaining parts of that meaning are insufficient. So it is known that this type of objection only occurs in composite causes consisting of two or more descriptions. And refutation by this type is conditioned upon the arguer's inability to bring a substitute for the description that the objector invalidated. If he mentions a valid substitute that can be a cause for the ruling, the objection is nullified and the proof is established. The invalidation of the part is by the objector's showing that it is irrelevant (mulgha) by the existence of the ruling when it is absent. And the meaning of 'insufficiency of the rest' is its lack of effect in the ruling. It has two forms: The first: that the arguer brings a substitute for the description that has been dropped from consideration. For example, he says concerning the obligation of performing the fear prayer (salat al-khawf): 'It is a prayer that must be made up if not performed, so its performance is obligatory by analogy to the security prayer (salat al-amn); just as it must be made up if not performed, so its performance is obligatory.' So the obligation of make-up is the cause, and the obligation of performance is the ruling caused by that cause. Then the objector says: 'The specificity of prayer in your evidence is irrelevant,' and he shows that Hajj is also obligatory in performance like make-up. So the arguer substitutes the specificity of prayer that the objector invalidated with a general description, which is worship ('ibadah), saying: 'It is an act of worship that must be made up if not performed...'
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage introduces another type of objection (kasar) in dialectical reasoning: invalidating part of a composite cause. The author explains that this type of objection only applies when the cause is composed of multiple descriptions (e.g., 'prayer' and 'obligation of make-up'). The objector may argue that one part (e.g., 'prayer') is not effective because the ruling (obligation of performance) exists even when that part is absent (e.g., Hajj also has obligation of make-up and performance, but Hajj is not prayer). This shows that 'prayer' is not a valid part of the cause. The author then states that if the arguer can replace the invalidated part with a valid substitute (e.g., replace 'prayer' with 'worship'), the objection is removed and the argument stands. The author gives a concrete example from the obligation of performing the fear prayer (salat al-khawf). The arguer's original cause includes 'prayer' and 'obligation of make-up'. The objector points out that 'prayer' is not exclusive; Hajj shares the same ruling. So the arguer substitutes 'prayer' with the broader category 'worship', which includes both prayer and Hajj, and thus the cause becomes 'act of worship that must be made up if not performed'. This then covers both prayers and Hajj, so the objection is overcome.

Invalidating part of a composite cause does not automatically break the argument if the arguer can provide a valid substitute that restores the cause's validity.

?

Dialectic -- Jadal

Kasar by Invalidating a Part of a Composite Cause
Author explains and exemplifies invalidation of a part of a multi-component cause in dialectical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the jadal technique of kasar by invalidating a part of a composite cause in dialectical argument.

Core Idea
A composite cause can be refuted by invalidating one part, forcing a substitute or collapse.

In dialectical debate, a proponent may present a composite cause (two or more attributes) to justify a ruling. The objector can attack by invalidating one part, making the cause deficient. This lesson explains the mechanics and two outcomes: the proponent either provides a substitute or leaves the cause incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Identify the composite cause in the proponent's argument
Look for the attributes (awṣāf) that together are claimed to be the ‘illa (cause) for the ruling. For example, in the prayer of fear argument, the cause includes ‘prayer’ and ‘obligation of makeup’.
A composite cause must have at least two attributes.
2
The objector invalidates one part
The objector shows that one attribute is not effective (mulghā) because the ruling exists without it. For instance, ‘prayer’ is not effective because Hajj also has obligatory performance without being a prayer.
Invalidation is done by counterexample or showing lack of correlation.
3
Check if the proponent can provide a substitute
If the proponent produces a valid substitute (badal) for the invalidated part, the argument may be saved. The substitute must be a suitable cause for the ruling.
If no substitute is offered, the cause collapses to a single attribute.
4
Test the substitute against further objection
The objector may refute the substitute as well. For example, the substitute ‘worship’ was refuted by the fast of a menstruating woman, which is worship but not obligatory to perform.
This shows the dialectical nature: each move invites a counter-move.
5
Determine the outcome
If the proponent cannot provide a suitable substitute or the substitute is also refuted, the argument fails. If a valid substitute withstands objection, the proof stands.
Success depends on the strength of the substitute and the objector's counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أنواع الكسر إبطال المعترض جزءا من المعنى المعلَّل به، ونقصُه ما تبقى من أجزاء ذلك المعنى المعلل به فعلم أن هذا النوع من الكسر لا يكون إلا في العلل المركبة من وصفين فأكثر . والقدح بهذا النوع مقيد بعجز المستدل عن الإتيان ببدل من الوصف الذي أبطله المعترض، فإن ذكر بدلاً صالحًا لأن يكون علة الحكم أُلغي الكسر واستقام الدليل . وإبطال الجزء بأن يبيّن المعترض أنه مُلغى بوجود الحكم عند انتفائه، والمراد بنقص الباقي عدم تأثيره في الحكم، وله صورتان : الأولى: أن يأتي المستدلِ ببدل الوصف المسقط عن الاعتبار، كأن يقول في وجوب أداء صلاة الخوف : هي صلاة يجب قضاؤها لو لم تفعل فيجب أداؤها قياسًا على صلاة الأمن؛ فإنها كما يجب قضاؤها لو لم تفعل يجب أداؤها، فوجوب القضاء هو العلة، ووجوب الأداء هو الحكم المعلل بتلك العلة. فيقولُ المعترض : خصوص الصلاة في دليلك مُلغَى، ويبين بأن الحج واجب الأداء كالقضاء. فيبدل المستدل خصوص الصلاة الذي أبطله المعترض بوصف عام وهو العبادة، فيقول : هي عبادة يجب قضاؤها لو لم تفعل، إلى آخره . فينقض عليه المعترض هذا البدل أيضًا بصوم الحائض ؛ فإنه عبادة يجب قضاؤها ولا يجب أداؤها، بل يحرم . الصورة الثانية : ألا يبدل المستدل الوصف الذي أبطله المعترض، فلا يبقى للمستدل علةٌ في المثال المذكور إلا قوله : يجب قضاؤها . فينقضه المعترض بأن يقول : ليس كلُّ ما يجب قضاؤه يجب أداؤه، بدليل صوم الحائض في رمضان ؛ فإنه عبادة يجب قضاؤها ولا يجب أداؤها، بل يحرم. ومثال الإتيان ببدل صالح للتعليل أن يقول مشترط النية في الوضوء : الوضوء طهارة واجبة فتشترط لها النية كغيرها من الواجبات . فيقول المعترض : خصوص الطهارة مُلغَى، لأنه لا أثر له في وجوب النية ؛ لأن طهارة الخبث طهارة لا تشترط لها النية إجماعا . فيُبْدِل لفظ الطهارة الذي أبطله المعترض بغيره فيقول : الوضوء قربة غير معقولة المعنى ؛ لأن موجَبَها في غير محل موجبها، فتشترط لها النية كسائر القرب التي هي ليست معقولة المعنى . وقصدنا المثال لا مناقشة أدلة الأقوال . وقد بيّنا فيما مضى أنا إن جئنا بالدليل على صورة القياس الأصولي كهذه الأمثلة التي ذكرنا آنفًا، فإنما نفعل ذلك لأنه في قوة قياس منطقي اقتراني بجعل الفرع حدا أصغر، والأصل حدا أكبر.
Among the types of refutation is the objector's invalidation of a part of the meaning being used as a cause, and the deficiency of the remaining parts of that meaning being used as a cause. It is known that this type of refutation occurs only in composite causes consisting of two or more attributes. The attack by this type is conditioned upon the inability of the proponent to produce a substitute for the attribute that the objector invalidated. If he mentions a substitute suitable to be a cause for the ruling, the refutation is nullified and the proof stands. Invalidation of the part is by the objector showing that it is discarded because the ruling exists without it. The meaning of 'deficiency of the remainder' is its lack of effect on the ruling. It has two forms: The first: the proponent brings a substitute for the attribute that has been removed from consideration. For example, regarding the obligation of performing the prayer of fear: 'It is a prayer whose makeup is obligatory if not performed, so its performance is obligatory by analogy with the prayer of safety; for just as its makeup is obligatory if not performed, its performance is obligatory.' So the obligation of makeup is the cause, and the obligation of performance is the ruling caused by that cause. The objector says: 'The specific nature of prayer in your proof is discarded,' and he shows that Hajj is obligatory in performance like makeup. So the proponent substitutes the specific nature of prayer that the objector invalidated with a general attribute, namely worship, and says: 'It is an act of worship whose makeup is obligatory if not performed,' etc. Then the objector refutes this substitute as well by the fast of a menstruating woman, for it is an act of worship whose makeup is obligatory but whose performance is not obligatory; rather, it is forbidden. The second form: The proponent does not substitute the attribute that the objector invalidated, so the proponent has no cause in the mentioned example except his statement: 'Its makeup is obligatory.' So the objector refutes him by saying: 'Not everything whose makeup is obligatory requires performance, as evidenced by the fast of a menstruating woman during Ramadan, for it is an act of worship whose makeup is obligatory but whose performance is not obligatory; rather, it is forbidden.' An example of bringing a suitable substitute for causation is when the one who requires intention in ablution says: 'Ablution is a required purification, so intention is required for it like other required acts.' The objector says: 'The specific nature of purification is discarded, because it has no effect on the obligation of intention, since purification from impurity is a purification for which intention is not required by consensus.' So he substitutes the word 'purification' which the objector invalidated with another, saying: 'Ablution is an act of devotion whose meaning is not rationally comprehensible, because its requirement is in a place other than the place of its requirement, so intention is required for it like other devotions that are not rationally comprehensible in meaning.' Our purpose is the example, not to critique the proofs of the opinions. We have already explained earlier that if we bring the proof in the form of an uṣūlī analogy like these examples we just mentioned, we do so only because it is equivalent in force to a conjunctive categorical syllogism, making the branch the minor term and the original the major term.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates a specific type of refutation (kasar) in dialectical argumentation (jadal). The author explains that one kind of refutation involves the objector (mu'tarid) invalidating a part of the composite cause ('illa murakkaba) that the proponent (mustadill) has advanced. After explaining the condition for this kind of attack—namely, the proponent's inability to provide a substitute—he presents two scenarios. In the first scenario, the proponent offers a replacement attribute, but the objector refutes that too with a counterexample. In the second scenario, the proponent fails to substitute, leaving only a single attribute that is then refuted. Both scenarios are illustrated with fiqh examples: the prayer of fear (salat al-khawf) and the fast of a menstruating woman (sawm al-ha'id). The author also gives an example involving intention (niyya) in ablution (wudu'), where the proponent initially uses 'purification' (tahara) and then substitutes it with 'devotion not rationally comprehensible in meaning' (qurba ghayr ma'qulat al-ma'na). Finally, the author notes that these examples are presented in the form of uṣūlī analogy (qiyas usuli), which is equivalent to a conjunctive categorical syllogism with the branch (far') as the minor term and the original (asl) as the major term. This bridges the dialectical method to formal logic.

al-'illa al-murakkaba
al-badal
al-mustadill
al-mu'tarid

The art of kasar in jadal allows the objector to dismantle a composite cause by invalidating one part, forcing the proponent to either provide a valid substitute or lose the argument.

The Second Scenario of Naqd in Jadal
Author explains the second scenario where the mudallil does not substitute the invalidated description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second scenario of naqd where the mudallil does not substitute an invalidated description, and analyse how the mu'tarid refutes the remaining 'illa using a counterexample from fiqh.

Core Idea
When the mudallil refuses to replace an invalidated cause, the mu'tarid can attack it directly with a counterexample.

This lesson examines a key dialectical move in Islamic legal argumentation. When a proponent's original cause is refuted, they may choose not to substitute it. The opponent then targets the remaining justification, seeking to show its inconsistency. You will see how a real fiqh example—menstruation and fasting—serves as a powerful refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mudallil's remaining claim
Find the single 'illa that remains after the original description was invalidated. In the passage, it is the statement: 'Its making up is obligatory.'
The mudallil has not replaced the invalidated description, so this is the only causal claim left.
2
Understand the mu'tarid's counterexample
The mu'tarid brings the example of a menstruating woman's fast in Ramadan. Although its qada' is obligatory, its performance during menstruation is forbidden. This directly contradicts the mudallil's implied claim that everything whose qada' is obligatory must be performed.
The counterexample shows that the 'illa (obligation of qada') does not consistently lead to the desired ruling (obligation of performance).
3
Analyse the logical structure of the naqd
The mu'tarid's objection is a naqd because it presents a case where the 'illa exists (qada' is obligatory) but the ruling (performance is obligatory) does not apply. This invalidates the 'illa as a proper cause.
This is a classic naqd move in jadal: showing a counterexample that violates the correlation between 'illa and ruling.
4
Apply the rule to a new fiqh scenario
Think of another act of worship where qada' is obligatory but performance is not. For example, missed prayers due to unconsciousness: qada' is obligatory, but the prayer at the time of unconsciousness is not required. Test if the same naqd would work against someone claiming performance is obligatory because qada' is.
This exercise reinforces how naqd functions as a refutation tool in legal debates.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصورة الثانية : ألا يبدل المستدل الوصف الذي أبطله المعترض، فلا يبقى للمستدل علةٌ في المثال المذكور إلا قوله : يجب قضاؤها . فينقضه المعترض بأن يقول : ليس كلُّ ما يجب قضاؤه يجب أداؤه، بدليل صوم الحائض في رمضان ؛ فإنه عبادة يجب قضاؤها ولا يجب أداؤها، بل يحرم.
The second picture: that the istidlal does not substitute the description which the mu'tarid invalidated, so nothing remains for the istidlal as a cause in the mentioned example except his statement: 'Its making up is obligatory.' Then the mu'tarid refutes this by saying: Not everything whose making up is obligatory is obligatory to perform, evidenced by the fasting of a menstruating woman in Ramadan, for it is an act of worship whose making up is obligatory but its performance is not obligatory, rather it is forbidden.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage describes the second scenario in naqd (refutation) concerning the use of an invalidated description (wasf) in a legal analogy (qiyas usuli). The author explains that when the istidlal (the one adducing the evidence) does not replace the description that the mu'tarid (objector) has invalidated, the only remaining cause (‘illa) in the example is a single statement: 'Its making up (qada') is obligatory.' The mu'tarid then refutes this by providing a counterexample: the fasting of a menstruating woman in Ramadan. This is an act of worship whose making up is obligatory but whose actual performance during menstruation is forbidden. Therefore, the claim that 'Everything whose making up is obligatory must be performed' is false. This shows that the specific ‘illa is not valid. The example is taken from the Islamic legal context regarding obligations of worship, illustrating the principle of naqd in jadal.

Substituting a Valid Badal in Dialectical Jadal
Author gives example of valid substitute and relates to categorical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a dialectical substitution (badal) is required and reconstruct the logical form of the resulting argument as a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
A dialectical substitution replaces an invalidated description with a valid cause, transforming the argument into a categorical syllogism.

In jadal, when the mu'tarid invalidates the mudallil's description, the mudallil must replace it with a valid 'illa (cause). This passage shows how the 'usuli qiyas' of the substitution can be mapped onto a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani). We'll walk through the example and the logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial argument and objection
The mudallil argues: 'Wudu' is an obligatory purification, so it requires intention like other obligatory acts.' The mu'tarid objects that 'tahara' (purification) is not a valid cause because taharat al-khabath (removal of filth) is also a purification but does not require intention by consensus.
2
Find the substitute description
The mudallil replaces 'tahara' with 'qurba ghayr ma'qulat al-ma'na' (a devotional act not rationally understood). This new attribute is chosen because wudu' is an act whose rationale is not fully grasped, and other similar acts require intention.
The substitute must be an attribute that is consistently linked to the ruling.
3
Understand why the substitute works
The objection to 'tahara' was based on a counterexample. The new description 'qurba ghayr ma'qulat al-ma'na' avoids that counterexample because taharat al-khabath is not a devotional act of that type. Thus the attribute is a valid 'illa.
4
Map the argument to a categorical syllogism
The author explains that usuli qiyas is equivalent to a categorical syllogism: the branch (wudu') is the minor term, the original (other devotional acts not rationally understood) is the major term, and the 'illa (the attribute 'not rationally understood') is the middle term. The syllogism is: Every devotional act not rationally understood requires intention; wudu' is such an act; therefore wudu' requires intention.
This mapping shows the logical rigor behind usuli analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال الإتيان ببدل صالح للتعليل أن يقول مشترط النية في الوضوء : الوضوء طهارة واجبة فتشترط لها النية كغيرها من الواجبات . فيقول المعترض : خصوص الطهارة مُلغَى، لأنه لا أثر له في وجوب النية ؛ لأن طهارة الخبث طهارة لا تشترط لها النية إجماعا . فيُبْدِل لفظ الطهارة الذي أبطله المعترض بغيره فيقول : الوضوء قربة غير معقولة المعنى ؛ لأن موجَبَها في غير محل موجبها، فتشترط لها النية كسائر القرب التي هي ليست معقولة المعنى . وقصدنا المثال لا مناقشة أدلة الأقوال . وقد بيّنا فيما مضى أنا إن جئنا بالدليل على صورة القياس الأصولي كهذه الأمثلة التي ذكرنا ،آنفًا ، فإننا إنما نفعل ذلك لأنه في قوة قياس منطقي اقتراني بجعل الفرع حدا أصغر، والأصل حدا أكبر، والعلة حدا أوسط، كما أوضحناه سابقا بأمثله (۱).
And an example of coming with a substitute valid for ta'lil: the one who stipulates intention (niyya) in wudu' says: 'Wudu' is a purification that is obligatory, so intention is required for it like other obligatory acts.' Then the mu'tarid says: 'The particularity of purification is nullified, because it has no effect on the obligation of intention; for the purification of filth is a purification that does not require intention by consensus.' So he substitutes the word 'purification' which the mu'tarid invalidated with another, saying: 'Wudu' is a devotional act (qurba) whose meaning is not rationally understood (ghayr ma'qulat al-ma'na), because its basis is in a place other than its own basis, so intention is required for it like other devotional acts that are not rationally understood.' And we aim for the example, not to discuss the evidence for opinions. And we have previously explained that if we bring the evidence in the form of usuli analogy like these examples we mentioned just now, we only do that because it is in the power of a categorical logical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi iqtirani) by making the branch (far') the minor term, the original (asl) the major term, and the cause ('illa) the middle term, as we clarified earlier with its examples.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents an illustrative exchange in the discipline of jadal (dialectics). The author first gives an example of a mudallil offering a substitute description (badal) after his original was invalidated. The topic is wudu' and the requirement of intention (niyya). The mudallil initially argues that wudu' is a purification (tahara) that is obligatory, so it requires intention like other obligatory acts. The mu'tarid objects that the particularity of 'purification' is irrelevant because the purification of filth (taharat al-khabath) is also a purification yet does not require intention by unanimous agreement. The mudallil then replaces the description 'purification' with 'a devotional act whose meaning is not rationally understood' (qurba ghayr ma'qulat al-ma'na). This is because the ruling of wudu' (removing it) is in a place different from its basis (raising it), indicating that the act's wisdom is not fully grasped, so intention is required as in other similar devotional acts like ablution before prayer and tayammum. The author emphasizes that this is merely an example and not a discussion of the actual evidence for the opinions on intention in wudu'. He then notes that these examples of 'usuli qiyas' (legal analogy) are in fact equivalent to categorical syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) in formal logic: the branch (far') is the minor term, the original (asl) is the major term, and the cause ('illa) is the middle term. This demonstrates how the structure of legal reasoning aligns with logical form.

Key Terms
بدل صالح للتعليل
طهارة
قربة غير معقولة المعنى
قياس أصولي
قياس منطقي اقتراني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example, why does the mu'tarid reject 'tahara' as a valid 'illa?
A) Because wudu' is not really a purification
B) Because taharat al-khabath is also a purification but does not require intention, showing that 'tahara' is not a consistent cause
C) Because intention is not required in any purification
D) Because the mudallil's statement was grammatically incorrect

Key Takeaway
A dialectical substitution must replace the invalidated attribute with a different attribute that is consistently linked to the ruling, and the resulting argument can be mapped onto a valid categorical syllogism.

The mu'tarid objects: 'Not all dispensations are permissible; e.g., shortening prayer is a dispensation but requires conditions.' Propose a valid substitute description for 'dispensation' that would salvage the argument, and then express the new argument as a categorical syllogism.

Distinguishing Naqd and Kasr: Two Defects in Legal Evidence
Defining and distinguishing naqd and kasr in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the usul definitions of naqd and kasr and explain how they relate to the broader jadal terminology.

Core Idea
Naqd and kasr are technical defects specific to usul, but grounded in jadal logic.

This passage from a classical usul work clarifies two defects that can invalidate legal evidence. The author takes care to define naqd and kasr precisely as they are used in usul al-fiqh, showing how these terms are narrower than their counterparts in the art of debate (jadal). Understanding this distinction is crucial for any student of Islamic legal theory, as it sharpens the ability to identify weaknesses in juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two defects
The author names two defects (qadih) in legal evidence: al-naqd and al-kasr. Locate these terms in the Arabic passage.
2
Extract the usul definition of naqd
Find where the author defines naqd specifically in the terminology of usul al-fiqh. Note that it is the failure of the ruling (hukm) to follow even when the cause ('illa) is present.
This is narrower than the jadal definition.
3
Compare usul naqd with jadal naqd
The author explains that in jadal, naqd applies to any failure of a consequent to follow from its evidence or any entailment of absurdity. In usul, it is restricted to the cause-hukm relationship. Understand the difference in scope.
The usul definition is a subset of the jadal definition.
4
Understand kasr and its resemblance to broken naqd
The second defect, kasr, involves invalidating part of the cause. The author likens it to 'broken naqd' in jadal, where the questioner deletes parts of the evidence. Note the parallel: both involve partial invalidation.
Kasr 'breaks' the integrity of the cause.
5
Synthesize the distinction
Summarize: naqd is about the ruling not following despite the cause being present, while kasr is about the cause itself being partially invalid. Both are defects but operate on different elements.
This distinction is critical for constructing and critiquing qiyas arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تكلمنا فيما مضى على قادحين من القوادح في الدليل عند أهل الأصول : الأول النقض، وبينا أن النقض في اصطلاح أهل الأصول [ داخل ](٢) في المراد بالنقض في فن البحث والمناظرة؛ لأنه في فن البحث والمناظرة صادق بتخلف كل مدلول عن دليله وكل استلزام للمحال (٣) كما تقدم إيضاحه (٤) . وفي اصطلاح أهل الأصول لا يطلق إلا على تخلف مدلول خاص علته تخلف الحكم عن في حال كونها موجودة؛ لأن عن دليله، وهو العلة دليل الحكم، ووجودها بدونه وجود للدليل بدون مدلوله، وأوضحنا كلام أهل الأصول فيه والأجوبة عنه . الثاني الكسر ، وإنما ذكرناه هنا في اصطلاح أهل الأصول وبينا صورة الجواب عنه على القول بأنه قادح لأنه له شبه بالنقض المكسور في البحث والمناظرة ؛ لأن النقض المكسور يَحْذِف فيه السائل بعض أجزاء الدليل، والكسر في فن الأصول يُبطل فيه المعترض بعض أجزاء العلة كما تقدم قريبًا .
We have already spoken previously about two defects among the defects in evidence according to the people of usul: the first is naqd, and we explained that naqd in the terminology of the people of usul is included in what is meant by naqd in the art of research and debate; because in the art of research and debate it applies to every failure of a consequent to follow from its evidence and every entailment of absurdity, as previously clarified. And in the terminology of the people of usul it is not applied except to the failure of a specific consequent from its evidence, which is the failure of the ruling to follow from the cause when the cause exists; because the cause is the evidence for the ruling, and the existence of the cause without the ruling is the existence of evidence without its consequent, and we have clarified the statements of the people of usul regarding it and the answers to it. The second is kasr, and we only mention it here in the terminology of the people of usul and we explained the form of the answer to it according to the opinion that it is a defect, because it resembles the 'broken naqd' in research and debate; for the broken naqd is when the questioner (sa'il) deletes some parts of the evidence, and kasr in the art of usul is when the mu'tarid invalidates some parts of the cause, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage discusses two defects (qadih) that can affect legal evidence according to the specialists in usul al-fiqh. The first is al-naqd (refutation). The author explains that the usul definition of naqd is more specific than that used in the discipline of debate (jadal). In jadal, naqd is general: it occurs whenever a consequent (the claimed conclusion) fails to follow from its evidence, or when an argument leads to an absurdity. However, in usul al-fiqh, naqd is specifically applied to the situation where the ruling (hukm) is absent despite the presence of the alleged cause ('illa). Since the cause is the evidence for the ruling, its existence without the ruling is like evidence without its consequent, which is invalid. The author mentions that he has already elaborated on the usul discussion and the possible responses to such naqd. The second defect is al-kasr (fracturing). The author brings it up because it has a resemblance to the 'broken naqd' (al-naqd al-maksur) in the field of debate. In a broken naqd, the questioner omits some parts of the opponent's evidence, resulting in a weakened counterargument. In usul, kasr is when the objector (mu'tarid) invalidates some parts of the cause itself, thereby breaking its coherence. The author notes that he has previously explained the form of the response to kasr according to the view that it is indeed a defect. This passage thus provides a clear distinction between the technical usages of these terms in jadal versus usul.

Key Terms
قادح
نقض
كسر
فن البحث والمناظرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the key difference between naqd in usul al-fiqh and naqd in the art of debate (jadal)?
A) In usul, naqd only applies when the cause is absent; in jadal, it is broader.
B) In usul, naqd is limited to cases where the ruling is absent despite the presence of the cause; in jadal, naqd includes any failure of evidence or entailment of absurdity.
C) In jadal, naqd requires a formal syllogism; in usul, it does not.
D) There is no difference; they are identical.

Key Takeaway
Naqd and kasr are two distinct defects in usul al-fiqh: naqd attacks the link between cause and ruling, while kasr attacks the coherence of the cause itself; both have roots in jadal but are more narrowly defined in usul.

Denying the Cause in the Original (Man' Wujud al-'Illa fi al-Asl)
Defines and exemplifies the objection of denying the claimed cause in the original case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the objection of 'denying the cause in the original' in an analogical argument (qiyas) and distinguish it from other objections.

Core Idea
The objector can defeat an analogy by proving the claimed cause does not exist in the original case.

In qiyas (analogical reasoning), the validity of extending a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') depends on the presence of a shared cause ('illa). One powerful objection is to deny that this cause actually exists in the original. This lesson unpacks that objection through concrete examples from Islamic legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qiyas structure
In the divorce example, locate the original (asl: 'So-and-so whom I marry is divorced'), the branch (far': 'If I marry so-and-so, she is divorced'), the ruling (hukm: divorce does not occur), and the claimed cause ('illa: conditional divorce of a foreign woman).
The 'illa is the shared attribute that supposedly justifies the ruling.
2
Examine the claimed cause in the original
Check whether the claimed 'illa (conditional divorce of a foreign woman) actually exists in the original. The objector (Maliki/Hanafi) argues it does not because the original statement is immediate, not conditional—there is no conditional particle.
This is the crux: the objector denies the existence of the attribute, not the ruling or the causal link.
3
Understand the objector's move
The objector is not disputing the ruling of the original, nor the causal link itself, but the factual presence of the described attribute in the original. This is called 'man' wujud al-'illa fi al-asl' (denying the existence of the cause in the original).
This differs from denying the causal effect (man' ta'thir) or denying the ruling (man' al-hukm).
4
Apply to the purity example
In the first example, the Hanafi denies the original ruling (dog skin not purified) altogether. But the focus is on the structure: the objector rejects the premise, so the qiyas cannot proceed. Compare this with the divorce example where the attribute itself is missing.
The purity example is simpler: the original ruling is directly denied, not just the 'illa.
5
Practice distinguishing objection types
Given a new qiyas, decide whether an objection is: (a) denial of the ruling, (b) denial of the 'illa's existence in the asl, or (c) denial of the 'illa's causal efficacy. Use the textbook's criteria.
This skill is crucial for constructing and deconstructing legal analogies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقول الحنفي : لا نسلّم حكم الأصل، وهو أن جلد الكلب لا يطهر بالدباغ، بل هو يطهر به عندي . ومثال منع وجود ما يدعيه علة في الأصل قول الشافعي والحنبلي : من قال إن تزوجت فلانة فهي طالق ثم تزوجها لم يلزمه طلاق ؛ قياسًا على قوله (فلانة التي أتزوجها طالق بجامع أن كلا منهما تعليق طلاقِ أجنبية، فالأصل المقيس عليه فلانة) التي أتزوجها طالق)، والفرع المقيس إن تزوجت فلانة فهي طالق والحكم هو عدم لزوم الطلاق بعد التزويج والعلة عند صاحب الدليل هي تعليق طلاق الأجنبية . فيقول المعترض كالمالكي والحنفي : الوصف الذي تدعي أنه علة الحكم وهو تعليق طلاق الأجنبية ليس موجودا أصلاً في الأصل المقيس عليه ؛ لأن الأصل الذي هو فلانة) التي أتزوجها طالق) تنجيز طلاق أجنبية، وهي لا يتنجز عليها الطلاق، والتعليق الذي زعمته علةً ليس موجودا أصلاً ، ولذا ليس في الصيغة أداة تعليق في الأصل. وهذا النوع الذي هو منع وجود ما يدعيه علة في الأصل هو المعروف بمركب الوصف. والحاصل في حكم هذه المسألة أن الأئمة الأربعة وغيرهم من فقهاء الامصار متفقون أن الطلاق لا يقع إلا بعد النكاح ؛ لأن الله رتبه عليه بثم في قوله: ﴿ يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا إِذَا نَكَحْتُمُ الْمُؤْمِنَاتِ ثُمَّ طَلَقْتُمُوهُنَّ ) [الأحزاب/ ٤٩] الآية، فإن قال إن تزوجت فلانة فهي طالق ثم تزوجها فالمالكي والحنفي يقولان بلزوم الطلاق؛ لأنه ما وقع إلا بعد النكاح، فقد راعيا وقت نفوذ الطلاق، وهي وقت نفوذه زوجة . والشافعي والحنبلي يقولان بعدم لزوم الطلاق؛ لأنهما نظرا إلى وقت صدور الصيغة منه ، وهي وقت صدور صيغة الطلاق المعلّق على التزويج أجنبية . أما تنجيز طلاق الأجنبية فهو لغو بلا خلاف .
So the Hanafi says: We do not accept the ruling of the original, which is that the skin of a dog is not purified by tanning; rather it is purified by it according to me. An example of denying the existence of what he claims as the cause in the original is the statement of the Shafi'i and Hanbali: Whoever says 'If I marry so-and-so, she is divorced,' then marries her, divorce does not befall him; analogically to his statement 'So-and-so whom I marry is divorced,' on the basis that each of them is a conditional divorce of a foreign woman. So the original measured upon is 'So-and-so whom I marry is divorced,' and the branch measured is 'If I marry so-and-so, she is divorced,' and the ruling is non-occurrence of divorce after marriage, and the cause according to the proponent is the conditional divorce of a foreign woman. So the objector, like the Maliki and Hanafi, says: The description you claim as the cause of the ruling, which is the conditional divorce of a foreign woman, does not exist at all in the original measured upon; because the original, which is 'So-and-so whom I marry is divorced,' is an immediate (unconditional) divorce of a foreign woman, and divorce is not immediately effective upon her, and the conditionality which you supposed as a cause does not exist at all, hence there is no conditional particle in the original. This type, which is denying the existence of what he claims as the cause in the original, is known as 'complex of the description' (murakkab al-wasf). The conclusion regarding the ruling of this issue is that the four Imams and other jurists of the regions are agreed that divorce does not occur except after marriage; because Allah arranged it after marriage with 'thumma' in His saying: 'O you who have believed, when you marry believing women and then divorce them...' [Al-Ahzab: 49]. So if he says 'If I marry so-and-so, she is divorced,' then marries her, the Maliki and Hanafi say divorce occurs, because it only occurred after marriage; they considered the time of effectiveness of divorce, which is when she is a wife. The Shafi'i and Hanbali say divorce does not occur, because they looked at the time of issuance of the statement from him, which is when the statement of divorce conditional on marriage was issued, she is a foreign woman. As for the unconditional divorce of a foreign woman, it is null and void without disagreement.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of objection in qiyas, called 'denying the existence of the claimed cause in the original' (man' wujud al-'illa fi al-asl), known in usul terminology as 'murakkab al-wasf'. The author provides two examples: the first is from purity jurisprudence, where the Hanafi denies that the dog's skin is not purified by tanning, directly denying the original ruling. The second, more detailed example is from divorce law: a Shafi'i/Hanbali argues that if someone says 'If I marry so-and-so, she is divorced' and then marries her, divorce does not occur, analogizing to the case where one says 'So-and-so whom I marry is divorced' (immediate divorce). The claimed cause (the 'illa) is that both involve the conditional divorce of an alien woman. The Maliki/Hanafi objector denies that this cause exists in the original, because the original statement is an immediate (unconditional) divorce of a foreign woman, not a conditional one; there is no conditional particle. The passage then clarifies the actual ruling: all four schools agree that divorce only occurs after marriage, but they differ on the timing: Maliki/Hanafi look at the time of effectiveness (after marriage, so it occurs), while Shafi'i/Hanbali look at the time of utterance (when uttered, she is a foreign woman, so the conditional is void). The unconditional divorce of a foreign woman is unanimously null.

Key Terms
منع وجود العلة في الأصل
مركب الوصف
العلة

A) Because the original statement is an immediate (unconditional) divorce, not conditional.
B) Because the original statement was not even a divorce.
C) Because the original statement referred to a married woman.
D) Because the original statement was made after marriage.

Key Takeaway
Denying the existence of the claimed cause in the original (man' wujud al-'illa fi al-asl) is a precise objection that stops an analogy by showing the shared attribute is not actually present in the original case.
