Denying the Description as Cause: Man' Kawn al-Wasf 'Illa
Explains the second type of objection in qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the objection of 'denying the description is the cause' in an analogical argument (qiyas) and distinguish it from denying the description's existence.

Core Idea
You can accept the description exists but still block the analogy by denying it is the cause.

This lesson covers the second major objection in qiyas: the opponent concedes that the description (wasf) is present in the original case (asl) but disputes its status as the effective cause ('illa). This move forces the debate to focus on what the true cause is, often resulting in what usul scholars call 'murakkab al-asl' (complex of the original). Understanding this objection sharpens your ability to challenge analogical reasoning in Islamic legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure
The objector admits the description exists but rejects it as the cause. This is different from denying the description's existence entirely.
2
Identify the example
In the riba example, the Shafi'i admits kayl (measurement) exists in wheat but denies it is the cause of prohibition; he claims the cause is ta'm (food substance).
The original claimant (e.g., Hanafi) holds kayl as the cause.
3
Understand the terminology
This situation is called 'murakkab al-asl' (complex of the original) because the cause is disputed between two possibilities.
Contrast with 'murakkab al-wasf' where the description itself is denied existence.
4
Memorize the definition verse
The verses from Maraqi al-Sa'ud succinctly define both terms: 'If they differ regarding two causes, the original is complex; the description becomes complex when the opponent denies its existence in the original.'
This aids in distinguishing the two related objections.
5
Apply the concept
In any qiyas, if the opponent concedes the description but challenges causation, you are dealing with man' kawn al-wasf 'illa. Practice spotting this move in fiqh examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما منع كون الوصف علة فهو أن يعترف المعترض بوجود الوصف في الأصل المقيس عليه، ولكنه يمنع كونه هو العلة . كأن يقول الشافعي : وصف الكيل موجود في البرّ، ولكنه ليس هو علة تحريم الربا فيه فإن ادعى الخصم له علة أخرى كقول الشافعي في هذا المثال بل علة الربا فيه الطعم، فهو المعروف في اصطلاح أهل الأصول بمركب الأصل، وقد عرف صاحب مراقي السعود (١) مركب الأصل [ هو و ] (٢) مركب الوصف بقوله : وإن يكن لعلتين اختلفا تركب الأصل لدى من سلفا تَركَّبَ مركب الوصفُ إذا الخصم منع وجودَ ذا الوصف في الاصل المتبع
As for denying that the description is the cause, it is that the objector acknowledges the existence of the description in the original measured upon, but he denies that it is the cause. For example, the Shafi'i says: The description of measuring (kayl) exists in wheat, but it is not the cause of the prohibition of usury in it. So if the opponent claims for it another cause, like the Shafi'i says in this example: 'Rather the cause of usury in it is taste (ta'm),' then this is known in the terminology of the scholars of usul as 'complex of the original' (murakkab al-asl). The author of Maraqi al-Sa'ud defined murakkab al-asl and murakkab al-wasf with his saying: 'And if they differ regarding two causes, the original is complex (murakkab al-asl) according to the predecessors; the description becomes complex (murakkab al-wasf) when the opponent denies the existence of that description in the original followed.'
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage introduces the second type of objection in qiyas: denying that a description (wasf) is the effective cause ('illa), while admitting its existence in the original. This is called 'murakkab al-asl' (complex of the original) in usul al-fiqh. The author gives the example of the prohibition of riba: the Shafi'i accepts that 'kayl' (measurement by volume) exists in wheat, but denies that it is the cause of riba. Instead, he claims the cause is 'al-ta'm' (food substance). This is a dispute over the identification of the cause. The passage then quotes two lines of verse from the famous poem 'Maraqi al-Sa'ud' by Ibn al-Sharif al-Tilimsani, which defines both terms: 'murakkab al-asl' when there is disagreement over two possible causes, and 'murakkab al-wasf' when the opponent denies the existence of the description in the original. Note that this lesson focuses on murakkab al-asl, which is the opposite of the previously discussed murakkab al-wasf.

Key Terms
منع كون الوصف علة
مركب الأصل
الربا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of riba in wheat, what does the Shafi'i deny?
A) That wheat is a foodstuff.
B) That measurement (kayl) exists in wheat.
C) That measurement is the cause of riba in wheat.
D) That wheat can be subject to riba.

Key Takeaway
Denying that the description is the cause is a powerful objection that forces the discussion to the core of why a ruling exists, leading to 'murakkab al-asl' when an alternative cause is proposed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an objection of man' kawn al-wasf 'illa for the qiyas: 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates; therefore, every intoxicant is forbidden.' The opponent admits wine intoxicates but denies intoxication is the cause (e.g., claims the cause is that it is an alcoholic beverage). Explain why this is not murakkab al-wasf.
?

Objection by Denying the Cause in the Branch (Man' Wujud al-'Illa fi al-Far')
Presents the third objection type: denying the claimed cause exists in the branch case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a valid objection by denying the existence of the claimed cause in the branch case of an analogical argument.

Core Idea
The objector does not reject the analogy; he denies that the cause exists in the branch.

In dialectical debate over qiyas, one powerful move is to concede the cause’s existence in the original case (asl) but deny it in the branch (far'). This is the third type of objection: man' wujud al-'illa fi al-far'. The example of the grave robber (nabbash) illustrates how a Hanafi objector might argue that the cause – theft from a protective place – does not apply to a grave, thus breaking the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the analogy
Locate the original case (asl), the branch case (far'), the cause (illa), and the ruling (hukm) in the passage.
Asl: thief stealing from a locked chest; far': grave robber (nabbash); illa: theft from a protective place (hirz); hukm: amputation of hand.
2
Recognize the majority's claim
The majority asserts that the cause exists in both asl and far', so the analogy holds.
They say the grave is a similar protective place (hirz mithl).
3
Understand the objector's move
The Hanafi objector does not dispute the cause in the asl, but denies its existence in the far'.
The objection targets the far' only, not the asl or the analogy itself.
4
Assess the basis for the denial
Analyze why the cause is denied: the grave may not qualify as a hirz equivalent to a locked chest.
This is a factual disagreement about the nature of the branch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال منع وجود الوصف الذي هو العلة في الفرع قول الجمهور : تُقطع يد النباش ؛ لأنه سرق الكفن من حرز مثله، قياسًا على السارق المُخرِج من الحرز ، كسرقته الدراهم من الصندوق المقفول . فيقول المعترض كالحنفي : وجود العلة التي هي السرقة ممنوع في ص
An example of denying the existence of the description that is the cause in the branch is the statement of the majority: The hand of the grave robber (nabbash) should be cut off because he stole the shroud from a similar protective place, analogically to the thief who takes out from a protective place, like stealing dirhams from a locked chest. So the objector, like the Hanafi, says: The existence of the cause, which is theft, is denied in ...
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage presents the third type of objection in qiyas: denying the existence of the claimed cause in the branch case (far'). The example given is the case of the grave robber (al-nabbash) who steals the shroud from the grave. The majority of scholars argue that his hand should be amputated, analogizing him to a thief who takes goods from a locked chest (a protected place). The cause is 'theft from a protected place' (sariqa min hirz). The Hanafi objector denies that this cause exists in the grave robber's case, because the grave may not be considered a protected place (hirz) in the same way as a locked chest. The passage is cut off at the end of the page, but the point is clear: the objection is a denial of the existence of the claimed cause in the branch.

Man' Kawn al-Wasf 'Illa and Tarkib al-Asl in Dialectical Objections
Author defines objection to causality of a descriptor and explains composite original.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the objection that a descriptor is not the cause (man' kawn al-wasf 'illa) and the composite original (tarkib al-asl) in a dialectical exchange, and apply the correct terminology to a usul al-fiqh example.

Core Idea
In jadal, admitting the descriptor's existence but denying its causality changes the argument structure.

In analogical reasoning (qiyas), an objector can challenge the causal link between the descriptor (wasf) and the ruling (hukm). One specific objection is 'man' kawn al-wasf 'illa' where the objector admits the descriptor exists in the original case but denies it is the effective cause. This leads to a situation called 'composite original' (tarkib al-asl) when the proponent claims a different cause. Mastering these distinctions is essential for constructing and responding to objections in munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
Read the definition: the objector admits the descriptor exists in the original (asl) but denies it is the 'illa. This is man' kawn al-wasf 'illa.
2
Analyze the example
In the Shafi'i example: wheat has the descriptor 'being measurable' (kayl). The Shafi'i admits this but says it is not the cause of riba; rather taste (ta'm) is the cause. This illustrates the objection.
3
Recognize tarkib al-asl
When the opponent (here the Shafi'i himself) claims a different cause, the situation is called 'composite original' because the asl now has two potential causes. The objector accepts the proponent's 'illa but offers an alternative.
4
Contrast with tarkib al-wasf
The poem explains tarkib al-wasf occurs when the objector denies the existence of the descriptor in the original. This is different from man' kawn al-wasf 'illa where the descriptor is admitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما منع كون الوصف علة فهو أن يعترف المعترض بوجود الوصف في الأصل المقيس عليه، ولكنه يمنع كونه هو العلة. كأن يقول الشافعي: وصف الكيل موجود في البرّ، ولكنه ليس هو علة تحريم الربا فيه. فإن ادعى الخصم له علة أخرى كقول الشافعي في هذا المثال بل علة الربا فيه الطعم، فهو المعروف في اصطلاح أهل الأصول بمركب الأصل، وقد عرف صاحب مراقي السعود مركب الأصل ومركب الوصف بقوله: وإن يكن لعلتين اختلفا تركب الأصل لدى من سلفا ترکب مركب الوصفُ إذا الخصم منع وجودَ ذا الوصف في الاصل المتبع.
As for objecting that the descriptor is the cause: it is that the objector acknowledges the existence of the descriptor in the original being analogized upon, but he prevents it from being the cause. For example, the Shafi'i says: the descriptor of measurement exists in wheat, but it is not the cause of prohibition of usury in it. If the opponent claims another cause for it, like the Shafi'i's statement in this example: 'Rather, the cause of usury in it is taste,' then it is known in the terminology of the scholars of usul as 'composite original' (tarkib al-asl). And the author of Maraqi al-Sa'ud defined composite original and composite descriptor with his saying: 'If it differs due to two causes, the original becomes composite according to those before. The composite descriptor occurs when the opponent prevents the existence of that descriptor in the original being followed.'
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage defines a specific type of objection in analogical reasoning (qiyas) within usul al-fiqh: the objection that a descriptor is not the cause (man' kawn al-wasf 'illa). The objector admits the descriptor exists in the original case (asl) but denies that it is the effective cause ('illa) of the ruling. The author gives an example from the Shafi'i school: wheat has the descriptor of being measurable, but the Shafi'i argues that measurability is not the cause of the prohibition of riba; rather, taste is the cause. When the opponent (here, the Shafi'i himself) claims a different cause, this situation is termed 'composite original' (tarkib al-asl) in usul terminology. The author then cites two lines of verse from the poem Maraqi al-Sa'ud by al-San‘ani, which define composite original (when the original case has two potential causes) and composite descriptor (when the opponent denies the existence of the descriptor in the original). The verses serve as a memorization aid for these concepts. The passage is foundational for dialectical training: it teaches how to formulate and respond to objections about causality, a key skill in munazara.

Key Terms
منع كون الوصف علة
مركب الأصل
مركب الوصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Man' kawn al-wasf 'illa is a specific objection where the descriptor is admitted but its causal link is denied, leading to a composite original when a different cause is claimed.

Parrying an Analogy: Man' Wujud al-Wasf in the Branch
Author exemplifies man' wujud al-wasf in a fiqh analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a man' wujud al-wasf objection in a fiqh analogy and distinguish it from other types of objections.

Core Idea
Denying the cause exists in the branch is a sharp surgical strike against the analogy.

In jadal (dialectical debate), a common move is to attack the analogy by claiming the 'illa (cause) is absent in the new case. This is called man' wujud al-wasf fi al-far'. The passage illustrates this with a classic Hanafi objection to cutting off a gravedigger's hand. By learning this, you will see how jurists protect their positions by refusing to grant the premise that the cause applies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's analogy
Find the original analogy: the majority analogize the gravedigger (nabbash) to a thief. What is the basis? Theft from custody (hirz).
The analogy is: thief is to cutting hand as gravedigger is to cutting hand.
2
Locate the claimed cause (wasf)
The cause claimed by the majority is 'sariqa' (theft). They assert the gravedigger steals the shroud from a secure place.
Cause = theft.
3
Find the objector's denial
The Hanafi objects by denying that the gravedigger is a thief. He argues the property is not stolen but is lost property (ma'a al-ḍayā'), so the cause 'theft' does not exist.
The denial is of the existence of the cause, not its causality.
4
Contrast with other objections
Contrast this with man' kawn al-wasf 'illa (admit existence but deny causality). Here, the Hanafi does not say theft cannot be a cause; he says there is no theft at all.
This distinction is crucial for accurate objection classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال منع وجود الوصف الذي هو العلة في الفرع قول الجمهور: تُقطع يد النباش؛ لأنه سرق الكفن من حرز مثله، قياسًا على السارق المُخرِج من الحرز، كسرقته الدراهم من الصندوق المقفول. فيقول المعترض كالحنفي: وجود العلة التي هي السرقة ممنوع في الفرع الذي هو النباش؛ لأن النباش ليس بسارق، بل آخذ مال عارض للضياع، كالملتقط.
And an example of objecting to the existence of the descriptor which is the cause in the branch is the saying of the majority: The hand of the gravedigger (nabbash) is cut off because he stole the shroud from a similar custody, by analogy to the thief who extracts from custody, like his theft of dirhams from a locked chest. So the objector, like the Hanafi, says: The existence of the cause, which is theft, is prevented in the branch, which is the gravedigger, because the gravedigger is not a thief; rather, he is a taker of property liable to loss, like a finder of lost property.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage provides a concrete example of the second type of objection: preventing the existence of the descriptor (wasf) in the branch case (far'). The descriptor here is 'theft' (sariqa), which is claimed to be the cause for cutting off the hand. The majority of scholars (jumhur) argue that the gravedigger's hand should be cut off because he stole the shroud from a protected place (hirz), analogizing him to an ordinary thief who steals a locked chest. The Hanafi objector challenges this by denying that the gravedigger is a thief. He argues that the gravedigger is not a thief because he takes property that is essentially lost (liable to be lost anyway), similar to someone who picks up lost property (multaqit). Thus, the descriptor 'theft' does not exist in the branch, so the analogy fails. This illustrates the mechanics of objection in jadal: the objector must pinpoint exactly which element of the analogy is contested. The example is drawn from Islamic criminal law (hudud) and shows how usul al-fiqh debates are grounded in substantive fiqh.

Key Takeaway
Man' wujud al-wasf fi al-far' is a dialectical tool that attacks the analogy by denying the existence of the claimed cause in the new case.

When Does Objecting to the Ruling of the Original Cut Off the Argument?
Author discusses scholarly disagreement on whether objecting to the ruling of the original ends the argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main positions on whether man' hukm al-asl cuts off the argument and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The proponent's right to defend the ruling of the original prevents debate from collapsing prematurely.

This passage tackles a crucial procedural question in munazara: if the objector simply rejects the ruling of the original case in an analogy, does that immediately end the proponent's argument? The answer shapes how debates unfold. The author walks through several scholarly views, each weighing the danger of infinite regress against the proponent's right to defend all premises. Let's dissect the dispute and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
The passage asks: when the objector says 'I object to the ruling of the original' (man' hukm al-asl), does that cut off the proponent (mustadill) from continuing his proof?
2
List the three positions
First: the proponent is not cut off (Ibn al-Hajib, Ibn Qudama). Second: he is cut off (some unnamed scholars). Third: it depends on local custom (al-Ghazali). Also note al-Shirazi's stricter view that the objection is not even heard.
Al-Shirazi's view is a variant of the first position.
3
Extract the justifications
For the first view: the proponent can still bring evidence for the ruling of the original. For the second: allowing such defense leads to topic expansion and possible infinite regress (tasalsul). For al-Ghazali: it's a conventional matter ('urf), not rational or revealed, so local practice decides.
Note the logical concern about tasalsul – a regress would make debates endless.
4
Analyze the author's stance
The author says 'the first is more apparent' (al-awwal azhar), favoring the view that the proponent is not cut off. But he reports al-Ghazali's view without rejecting it, suggesting it is an acceptable alternative.
5
Apply to a real debate
Consider a qiyas debate: the proponent argues that nabidh is haram because it resembles wine in intoxication. Objector: 'I deny that wine is intoxicating.' According to the dominant view, the proponent can now adduce evidence for wine's intoxication, and the debate continues.
This shows the practical impact of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه اختلف في توجيه المنع على حكم الأصل: هل ينقطع به المستدل أو لا؟ وأظهر القولين أنه لا ينقطع بمجرد منع حكم الأصل، وممن اختار ذلك ابن الحاجب وابن قدامة في روضة الناظر، وقالا إنه له إقامة الدليل على حكم الأصل الذي منعه المعترض. وقال بعضهم: ينقطع بذلك، ووجهوا قولهم أنه لو جاز للمستدل إقامة الدليل على حكم الأصل الذي منعه المعترض لانتشر الكلام وانتقلا من مسألة إلى أخرى، وقد يفضي ذلك إلى التسلسل، والأول أظهر. واختار الغزالي اتباع عرف البلد، فإن كان عرفهم في المناظرة بمجرد منع حكم الأصل انقطع به، وإلا فلا؛ لأنه أمر اصطلاحي، ليس بعقلي ولا شرعي. وقال أبو إسحاق الشيرازي: لا يُسمع منعه حكم الأصل، فلا ينقطع به.
And know that there is disagreement regarding the effect of objecting to the ruling of the original: does the proponent (mustadill) become cut off by it or not? The more apparent of the two opinions is that he is not cut off by mere objection to the ruling of the original. Among those who chose this are Ibn al-Hajib and Ibn Qudama in Rawdat al-Nazir, and they said that he has the right to provide evidence for the ruling of the original that the objector objected to. And some said: he is cut off by that. They justified their statement that if it were permissible for the proponent to provide evidence for the ruling of the original that the objector objected to, then speech would expand and they would move from one issue to another, and that may lead to infinite regress. The first is more apparent. And al-Ghazali chose following the custom of the town: if their custom in debate is that by mere objection to the ruling of the original he is cut off, then so it is; otherwise not, because it is a conventional matter, not intellectual or legal. And Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi said: his objection to the ruling of the original is not heard, so he is not cut off by it.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage addresses a procedural question in dialectical method (adab al-bahth): when an objector rejects the ruling of the original case (hukm al-asl) in an analogy, does that immediately defeat the proponent's argument? The author presents three views. The dominant view (al-azhar) is that the proponent is not cut off; he can defend the ruling of the original by adducing evidence. This is supported by Ibn al-Hajib and Ibn Qudama. The opposing view argues that allowing such defense leads to expanding the discussion beyond the original issue, potentially causing infinite regress (tasalsul). However, the author sides with the first view. Al-Ghazali introduces a contextual perspective: the ruling depends on the customary practice of debate in that locale, since it is a conventional (istilahi) matter, not based on reason or revelation. Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi takes a stricter stance: the objection to the ruling of the original is simply not entertained, so the proponent is never cut off. This passage is crucial for understanding the practical rules of debate: it teaches that the objector's role is limited, and the proponent has the right to defend all premises of his analogy. The debate about infinite regress also shows awareness of logical consequences.

Key Terms
منع حكم الأصل
استدلال
عرف البلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Does Preventing the Original Ruling Cut Off the Argument?
Scholarly disagreement on whether prevention of original ruling ends argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the different scholarly positions on whether man' hukm al-asl ends the disputation and explain the rationale for each.

Core Idea
The dominant view in jadal: the responder can prove the original ruling even if the objector denies it.

In analogical argumentation (qiyas), the objector may deny the ruling of the original case (man' hukm al-asl). But does this denial automatically end the argument? This lesson examines the scholarly debate on this procedural question, a key point in the etiquette of disputation (adab al-bahth). Understanding the rules prevents debates from stalling or degenerating into infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main opinions
The passage presents two opposing views: some scholars say the responder (mustadill) is cut off by man' al-asl, while others say he is not and may present evidence for the original ruling.
Ibn al-Hajib and Ibn Qudama represent the dominant view (al-azhar) that the responder is not cut off.
2
Analyze the arguments for each view
Those who say the responder is cut off argue that allowing proof would lead to spreading the discussion and possibly infinite regress. The dominant view holds that the responder must have the opportunity to defend the original ruling, otherwise the objection would be a mere blocking tactic.
3
Examine al-Ghazali's compromise
Al-Ghazali makes the effect dependent on local custom ('urf al-balad): if the custom in that disputation circle is that man' al-asl ends the argument, then it does; otherwise not. He calls it a conventional matter, not rational or legal.
This introduces a sociological element into procedural rules.
4
Consider al-Shirazi's extreme view
Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi says that such prevention is not even heard, so the responder is not required to respond. Ibn al-Hajib calls this far-fetched, and the author agrees.
This shows the range of strictness in jadal rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الذي هو النباش؛ لأن النباش ليس بسارق، بل آخذ مال عارض للضياع، كالملتقط .
واعلم أنه اختلف في توجيه المنع على حكم الأصل : هل ينقطع به المستدل أو لا؟
وأظهر القولين أنه لا ينقطع بمجرد منع حكم الأصل، وممن اختار ذلك ابن الحاجب وابن قدامة في روضة الناظر ، وقالا إنه له إقامة الدليل على حكم الأصل الذي منعه المعترض .
وقال بعضهم : ينقطع بذلك، ووجهوا قولهم أنه لو جاز للمستدل إقامة الدليل على حكم الأصل الذي منعه المعترض لانتشر الكلام وانتقلا من مسألة إلى أخرى، وقد يفضي ذلك إلى التسلسل، والأول أظهر.
واختار الغزالي اتباع عرف البلد، فإن كان عرفهم في المناظرة بمجرد منع حكم الأصل انقطع به، وإلا فلا؛ لأنه أمر اصطلاحي، ليس بعقلي ولا شرعي.
وقال أبو إسحاق الشيرازي : لا يُسمع منعه حكم الأصل، فلا يلزمه الاستدلال عليه. قال ابن الحاجب وهو بعيد. وقد صدق في ذلك.
وإذا علمت أن الأظهر عدم انقطاعه بذلك فاعلم أن جواب المستدل عن منع حكم الأصل هو إثباته [حكمه] بدليل .
والجواب عن منعه وجود الوصف في الأصل هو إثبات وجوده فيه بما هو طريق ثبوت مثله ؛ لأن الوصف قد يكون حسيا فيثبته بالحس، وقد يكون عقليّا فيثبته بالعقل، وقد يكون شرعيا فيثبته بالشرع .
ومثال الثلاثة المذكورة ما لو قال في القتل بالمثقل : قتل عمد عدوان، فلو قيل لا نسلم أنه قتل أجاب عنه بأن القتل ثابت بالحس الذي هو رؤيته بالحاسة، ولو قيل (لا) نسلم أنه عمد) فالجواب بأن العقل علم أنه عمد بالقرائن والأمارات المحتفّة به، التي لا تترك لبسًا عمد، وإن قيل لا نسلم أنه عدوان فالجواب بأن الشرع حرّمه من العدوان . وجعله في أنه .
والجواب عن منع كونه علة بإثبات كونه علة، كما لو قال المالكي للشافعي : أمنعُ كون الطَّعم علةَ الربا في البر. فيثبت الشافعي كونه علة، كأن يقول مثلاً : ملء كف من البر فيه الربا، وهو غير مكيل ولا مقتات ولا مدخر ؛ لقلته ولكنه مطعوم، فعلم استقلال الطعم بالعلية دون الأوصاف الأخرى.
The branch which is the grave-robber, because the grave-robber is not a thief, but rather a taker of property prone to loss, like a finder. And know that they differed concerning the justification of the prevention of the original ruling: does the deducer get cut off by it or not? The more apparent of the two opinions is that he is not cut off merely by the prevention of the original ruling. Among those who chose that are Ibn al-Hajib and Ibn Qudama in Rawdat al-Nazir, and they said that he has the right to adduce evidence for the original ruling that the objector prevented. And some said: he is cut off by that. They justified their statement that if it were permissible for the deducer to adduce evidence for the original ruling that the objector prevented, then speech would spread and they would move from one issue to another, and that may lead to infinite regress. The first is more apparent. Al-Ghazali chose following the custom of the city: if their custom in disputation is that merely preventing the original ruling cuts him off, then it does, otherwise not; because it is a matter of convention, not rational or legal. Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi said: his prevention of the original ruling is not heard, so he is not required to adduce evidence for it. Ibn al-Hajib said it is far-fetched. And he [Ibn al-Hajib] was correct in that. And if you know that the more apparent is that he is not cut off by that, then know that the deducer's answer to the prevention of the original ruling is to establish it by evidence. And the answer to his prevention of the existence of the description in the original is to establish its existence in it by whatever is the means of establishing something like it; because the description may be sensory, so he establishes it by sense, or intellectual, so he establishes it by intellect, or legal, so he establishes it by law. An example of the three mentioned is what if he said concerning killing with a heavy object: "intentional killing transgression." If one says "we do not accept that it is killing," he answers by saying that the killing is established by sense, which is seeing it with the sensory faculty. If one says "we do not accept that it is intentional," the answer is that the intellect knows it is intentional through the surrounding circumstances and indicators that leave no doubt concerning intentionality. If one says "we do not accept that it is transgression," the answer is that the law has made it transgression and considered it as such. And the answer to his prevention of it being a cause is by establishing it as a cause, as when the Maliki says to the Shafi'i: "I prevent [the claim that] taste is the cause of riba in wheat." So the Shafi'i establishes it as a cause, such as saying: a handful of wheat contains riba, and it is neither measured by volume nor staple nor stored due to its smallness, but it is edible, so it is known that taste alone is sufficient for causality without the other descriptions.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion about the rules of objection (i'tirad) in disputation, specifically regarding the prevention of the original ruling (man' al-asl). The author presents differing scholarly opinions on whether the objector's denial of the original ruling (i.e., the ruling in the case being analogized from) cuts off the argument of the responder (mustadill). The dominant view (al-azhar) is that it does not cut off the argument; the responder is allowed to prove the original ruling by evidence. This is the view of Ibn al-Hajib and Ibn Qudama. Those who say it does cut off argue that allowing proof would lead to endless expansion and infinite regress. Al-Ghazali opts for following local custom in disputation, as the matter is conventional, not rational or legal. Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi holds that such prevention is not even heard, so the responder need not respond. The author sides with Ibn al-Hajib that this is far-fetched. After clarifying that the responder is not cut off, the author explains how the responder answers three types of objections: (1) prevention of the original ruling: by proving it with evidence; (2) prevention of the existence of the description (wasf) in the original: by establishing the description through sense, intellect, or law, depending on the nature of the description; (3) prevention of the description being the cause ('illa): by proving its causality. Examples from fiqh are given: the case of killing with a heavy object (muthaqqal) demonstrates the three types of proofs for the description, and the Maliki-Shafi'i debate on riba in wheat illustrates proving causality. The author emphasizes that the responder must establish the causal connection independently.

Key Terms
منع حكم الأصل
مستدل
معترض
عرف البلد
تسلسل

Checkpoint
What is the position of Ibn al-Hajib and Ibn Qudama regarding the effect of preventing the original ruling?
A) The responder is cut off immediately.
B) The responder may adduce evidence for the original ruling.
C) The matter depends on local custom.
D) The objection is not even heard.

Key Takeaway
The more apparent view in jadal is that the responder is not cut off by man' hukm al-asl and may prove the original ruling, preventing arbitrary termination of arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a hypothetical debate about the prohibition of marijuana by qiyas on khamr, the objector says 'I deny that khamr is prohibited' (man' hukm al-asl). According to the dominant view in this passage, can the responder continue? What must he do? Write a short exchange of one objection and one response following the rules discussed.

Compare al-Ghazali's custom-based approach with Ibn al-Hajib's absolute approach. In a modern fiqh academy, which approach would be more practical for regulating debates? Justify your answer.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Three Objections to Analogical Arguments and Their Answers
Three types of objections to qiyas and how to answer them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of objections to qiyas (prevention of original ruling, prevention of existence of the description, prevention of causality) and state how the responder answers each, using the fiqh examples provided.

Core Idea
Three ways to object to an analogy: deny the original ruling, deny the description in the original, or deny the description is the cause.

Once we know that the responder is not automatically cut off by man' hukm al-asl, we need to know exactly how he responds to each type of objection. The passage classifies objections into three categories and provides a method of proof for each: sense, reason, or law. This lesson walks through these responses with concrete fiqh examples from the text, enabling you to apply them in real debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of objection
The objector may deny: (1) the original ruling (hukm al-asl), (2) the existence of the description (wasf) in the original, or (3) that the description is the cause ('illa). These correspond to the three pillars of analogical argument: the original ruling, the shared attribute, and the causal connection.
In the passage, these are referred to as 'man' hukm al-asl', 'man' wujud al-wasf fi al-asl', and 'man' kawnihi 'illa'.
2
Learn the general method of proof for the description
The description may be proved by sense (al-hiss), intellect (al-'aql), or law (al-shar'). The passage gives the example of killing with a heavy object (al-qatl bi al-muthaqqal): the fact of killing is sensed, the intentionality is inferred by intellect, and the transgression is determined by law.
This tripartite division of proofs is standard in usul al-fiqh and jadal.
3
Analyze the example of causality proof
The passage presents a Maliki- Shafi'i debate on riba in wheat. The Maliki denies that taste is the cause of riba. The Shafi'i proves it by pointing to a handful of wheat that is not measured or stored but is edible, showing that taste alone suffices for the ruling of riba.
This is an example of demonstrating causality by eliminating other potential attributes (sabr wa taqsim).
4
Apply the pattern to a new scenario
Given a new analogical argument, identify which type of objection is raised and then determine the appropriate response: prove the original ruling by evidence, prove the description by sense/reason/law, or prove the causal connection by elimination or direct evidence.
Practice with the checkpoint or apply_the_tool tasks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الذي هو النباش؛ لأن النباش ليس بسارق، بل آخذ مال عارض للضياع، كالملتقط .
واعلم أنه اختلف في توجيه المنع على حكم الأصل : هل ينقطع به المستدل أو لا؟
وأظهر القولين أنه لا ينقطع بمجرد منع حكم الأصل، وممن اختار ذلك ابن الحاجب وابن قدامة في روضة الناظر ، وقالا إنه له إقامة الدليل على حكم الأصل الذي منعه المعترض .
وقال بعضهم : ينقطع بذلك، ووجهوا قولهم أنه لو جاز للمستدل إقامة الدليل على حكم الأصل الذي منعه المعترض لانتشر الكلام وانتقلا من مسألة إلى أخرى، وقد يفضي ذلك إلى التسلسل، والأول أظهر.
واختار الغزالي اتباع عرف البلد، فإن كان عرفهم في المناظرة بمجرد منع حكم الأصل انقطع به، وإلا فلا؛ لأنه أمر اصطلاحي، ليس بعقلي ولا شرعي.
وقال أبو إسحاق الشيرازي : لا يُسمع منعه حكم الأصل، فلا يلزمه الاستدلال عليه. قال ابن الحاجب وهو بعيد. وقد صدق في ذلك.
وإذا علمت أن الأظهر عدم انقطاعه بذلك فاعلم أن جواب المستدل عن منع حكم الأصل هو إثباته [حكمه] بدليل .

The Four Objections in Qiyas and Their Responses
Explains the four types of objections in analogical reasoning and how to answer each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four objections to a qiyas (analogical argument) in usul al-fiqh and construct the appropriate response.

Core Idea
Every qiyas can be attacked at four points; the response is to prove the denied element.

In Islamic legal reasoning (qiyas usuli), an opponent may object to the analogy by denying one of its essential components. Ibn al-Hajib's text outlines four such objections and how the proponent must answer each. This lesson unpacks those four objections and their responses, using concrete examples from Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the four components of qiyas
Identify the original case (asl), the ruling of the original (hukm al-asl), the attribute (wasf) proposed as the effective cause ('illa), and the branch case (far'). The opponent can deny any of these.
These correspond to the four types of man'.
2
First objection: Deny the ruling of the original
The opponent says the original case does not have the claimed ruling. The response: prove that ruling with its own evidence, independent of the analogy.
Example: In killing with a blunt instrument, if the opponent denies it is killing, the proponent proves it by sensory evidence.
3
Second objection: Deny the existence of the attribute in the original
The opponent says the attribute does not exist in the asl. The response: prove its existence using the appropriate method: sensory for physical attributes, intellectual for mental ones, or legal for shar'i attributes.
Example: Intent is proven by intellectual signs; aggression by legal texts.
4
Third objection: Deny that the attribute is the effective cause
The opponent says the attribute is not the 'illa. The response: prove that the attribute alone is sufficient for the ruling, often by showing that the ruling applies even when other attributes are absent.
Example: Taste alone causes riba in wheat, as shown by the hadith of food exchange.
5
Fourth objection: Deny the existence of the cause in the branch
The opponent says the attribute does not exist in the far'. The response: prove its existence by analogy or direct evidence, showing that the far' shares the attribute.
Example: The grave is a safeguard (hirz) for the shroud, just as a stable is for animals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن الحاجب وهو بعيد . وقد صدق في ذلك وإذا علمت أن الأظهر عدم انقطاعه بذلك فاعلم أن جواب المستدل عن منع حكم الأصل هو إثباته حكمه بدليل . والجواب عن منعه وجود الوصف في الأصل هو إثبات وجوده فيه بما هو طريق ثبوت مثله؛ لأن الوصف قد يكون حسيا فيثبته بالحس، وقد يكون عقليّا فيثبته بالعقل، وقد يكون شرعيا فيثبته بالشرع . ومثال الثلاثة المذكورة ما لو قال في القتل بالمثقل : قتل عمد عدوان، فلو قيل لا نسلم أنه قتل أجاب عنه بأن القتل ثابت بالحس الذي هو رؤيته بالحاسة، ولو قيل لا نسلم أنه عمد فالجواب بأن العقل علم أنه عمد بالقرائن والأمارات المحتفّة به، التي لا تترك لبسًا عمد، وإن قيل لا نسلم أنه عدوان فالجواب بأن الشرع حرّمه من العدوان . والجواب عن منع كونه علة بإثبات كونه علة، كما لو قال المالكي للشافعي : أمنعُ كون الطَّعم علةَ الربا في البر. فيثبت الشافعي كونه علة، كأن يقول مثلاً : ملء كف من البر فيه الربا، وهو غير مكيل ولا مقتات ولا مدخر ؛ لقلته ولكنه مطعوم، فعلم استقلال الطعم بالعلية دون الأوصاف الأخرى. وكأن يقول الشافعي في المثال المذكور حديث معمر بن عبدالله عند مسلم قال : كنت أسمع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : «الطعام بالطعام مثلاً» الحديث (۱) ، يدل بمسلك الإيماء والتنبيه على أن الطعم هو علة الربا في المطعومات وقصدنا مطلق المثال لا مناقشة أدلة الأقوال . والجواب عن منع وجود العلة في الفرع هو إثبات وجودها فيه . كقول الجمهور الحرز في السرقة يتنوع بتنوع المسروق، فالإصطبل حرز للدواب وليس حرزا للدنانير، والصندوق حرز للدنانير وليس حرزاً للدواب، وبذلك التنوع يُعلم أن القبر حرز للكفن؛ لأنه حرز مثله، فالعلة التي هي السرقة موجودة في الفرع الذي هو النباش . وقد علمت أن المنع في الأصول كالمنع في البحث والمناظرة؛ لأن صاحب البحث والمناظرة يقول : أمنع مقدمة هذا الدليل مثلاً، والمعترض في الأصول يقول : أمنعُ حكم الأصل مثلاً ، أو وجود العلة فيه، أو كونها علة، أو وجودها في الفرع. وقد علمت مما مر أن القياس الأصولي راجع للقياس المنطقي بالطريق التي أوضحناها سابقا مرارًا (۲) ، فإذا رددت قياس الأصول إلى القياس المنطقي علمت أن مواقع المنع الأربعة في القياس الأصولي راجعة إلى منع مقدمات الدليل المنطقي . فلو قال الشافعي مثلاً : يحرم الربا في التفاح قياسًا على البر بجامع (۱) صحيح مسلم برقم (١٥٩٢). (۲) راجع ص۲۹۱.
Ibn al-Hajib said: 'It is far-fetched.' And he spoke the truth in that. And if you know that the apparent view is that it is not interrupted by that, then know that the answer of the one presenting the evidence (al-mustadill) to the objection (man‘) of the ruling of the original case (hukm al-asl) is to prove its ruling with evidence. And the answer to the objection of the existence of the attribute (wasf) in the original is to prove its existence in it by whatever is the way of confirming something like it; because the attribute may be sensory, so he confirms it by sense, may be intellectual, so he confirms it by intellect, and may be legal, so he confirms it by law. An example of the three mentioned is if one said regarding killing with a blunt instrument: 'It is intentional killing with aggression.' If it is said: 'We do not accept that it is killing', he answers that killing is established by sense, which is seeing it with the sense. If it is said: 'We do not accept that it is intentional', the answer is that the intellect knows that it is intentional by the indications and signs surrounding it, which leave no doubt about intent. And if it is said: 'We do not accept that it is aggression', the answer is that the law has prohibited it as aggression. And the answer to the objection of its being an effective cause (‘illa) is by proving that it is an effective cause, as when a Maliki says to a Shafi‘i: 'I object to taste being the effective cause of riba in wheat.' So the Shafi‘i proves that it is an effective cause, as if he says for example: 'A handful of wheat involves riba, and it is not measured by volume nor weighed nor stored due to its small quantity, but it is edible; so it is known that taste alone is the effective cause, to the exclusion of the other attributes.' And as if the Shafi‘i says in the aforementioned example the hadith of Ma‘mar ibn ‘Abdillah in Muslim, who said: 'I heard the Messenger of Allah ﷺ say: “Food for food, equal.”' The hadith (1) indicates, by way of allusion and intimation, that taste is the effective cause of riba in edible items. Our intent is absolute exemplification, not to discuss the evidences of opinions. And the answer to the objection of the existence of the effective cause in the branch (far‘) is to prove its existence in it. For example, the majority's statement: 'Protective custody (hirz) in theft varies according to the variety of the stolen item; the stable is a safeguard for animals but not for dinars, and the chest is a safeguard for dinars but not for animals. By that variation, it is known that the grave is a safeguard for the shroud because it is a safeguard of a similar kind. So the effective cause, which is theft, exists in the branch, which is the grave robber.' And you have known that objection in usul is like objection in investigation (bahth) and debate (munazara); because the person of investigation and debate says: 'I object to the premise of this evidence for example', and the objector in usul says: 'I object to the ruling of the original for example, or the existence of the effective cause in it, or its being an effective cause, or its existence in the branch.' And you have known from what has passed that analogical reasoning in usul (qiyas usuli) is reducible to logical syllogism (qiyas mantiqi) by the way we have clarified repeatedly before (2). So if you reduce the usul analogy to logical syllogism, you know that the four places of objection in usul analogy are reducible to objection to the premises of the logical proof. So if the Shafi‘i says for example: 'Riba is prohibited in apples by analogy to wheat, because of the commonality (1) Sahih Muslim, no. 1592. (2) See page 291.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the dialectical aspects of analogical reasoning (qiyas) in usul al-fiqh, specifically focusing on the four ways an opponent can object (man‘) to a qiyas and how the proponent (mustadill) must respond. The author begins by citing Ibn al-Hajib's statement that a certain view is far-fetched, and agrees with him. Then he explains that the proper response to each objection is to prove the denied element. The first objection is denial of the ruling of the original case (hukm al-asl); the answer is to prove that ruling with its own evidence. The second objection is denial of the existence of the attribute (wasf) in the original; the answer is to prove its existence according to its type: sensory attributes by sense, intellectual by intellect, and legal by law (shar‘). The author provides a concrete example from the case of killing with a blunt instrument (al-muthqal): if one claims it is intentional aggression, the opponent may deny the killing, the intent, or the aggression. Each is proven respectively by sense (witnessing the act), intellect (circumstantial evidence), and law (prohibition). The third objection is denial that the attribute is the effective cause (‘illa); the answer is to prove its causality, for instance by showing that it alone is sufficient for the ruling, as in the case of taste (ta‘m) in wheat for riba. The Shafi‘i argues that even a small handful of wheat, which is not measured by volume or weight, still involves riba, proving that taste alone is the cause. He also cites the hadith of food exchanged equally, which indicates by implication that taste is the cause. The fourth objection is denial of the existence of the ‘illa in the branch (far‘); the answer is to prove its existence, as in the example of the grave robber (nabbash) and the safeguard (hirz). The majority hold that hirz varies with the item: a stable is for animals, a chest for coins. By analogy, a grave is a safeguard for a shroud, so the ‘illa (theft) exists in the branch. Finally, the author relates these four usul objections to logical objections in debating (bahth wa munazara): in logic, the opponent says 'I deny a premise'; in usul, he says 'I deny the ruling of the original, or the existence of the ‘illa in it, or its being the ‘illa, or its existence in the branch.' The passage concludes that the usul qiyas is reducible to logical syllogism, and thus the four objections correspond to denying premises of the logical proof. A further example is given: the Shafi‘i may say riba is prohibited in apples by analogy to wheat due to the common attribute (probably taste). All examples are from Islamic legal reasoning, demonstrating the integration of logical principles into usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms

حكم الأصل
الوصف
العلة
الفرع
القياس الأصولي
القياس المنطقي

C) By quoting the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
The four objections in usul qiyas are reducible to denying premises in a logical syllogism, and each has a specific method of proof.

Mu'arada as Qalb: Turning the Opponent's Evidence Against Him
Defines mu'arada as qalb and gives a usul al-fiqh example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct a qalb objection in usul al-fiqh debates.

Core Idea
The objector uses the same evidence as the claimant to derive the opposite ruling.

In Islamic dialectic, a powerful technique is qalb (reversal): using the opponent's own proof to establish the opposite conclusion. This lesson unpacks the definition and a concrete example from the debate on bay' al-fuduli (sale by an unauthorized person) between Shafi'i and the Maliki/Hanafi schools. Understanding qalb sharpens your ability to spot when an objection is a genuine reversal rather than a mere denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's evidence and ruling
Shafi'i argues that bay' al-fuduli is invalid by analogy to the unauthorized purchase (shira' al-fuduli). His evidence: the purchase is valid only if the named person consents; otherwise it is binding on the unauthorized person. So the contract lacks full validity. His ruling: the sale is invalid.
2
Extract the implicit principle in the evidence
The principle in Shafi'i's evidence is: a contract concerning another's right without authority is either valid only upon consent or binding on the unauthorized person. This principle contains a conditional validity.
3
See how the objector uses the same principle to reach the opposite ruling
The Maliki/Hanafi objector says: The very same evidence shows that the purchase is valid for the named person upon consent, and if not, it is binding on the unauthorized person. Hence, the contract is valid in all cases. So the sale, by the same analogy, is valid. The objector does not deny the evidence; he uses it to prove that the ruling is the opposite (valid).
4
Verify this is qalb, not simple denial
Simple denial (man') would reject the analogy or its application. Here, the objector accepts the analogical reasoning but derives the opposite conclusion, thus reversing the proof. That is qalb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعارضة وأقسامها اعلم أن ما يسمى في البحث والمناظرة بالمعارضة على سبيل القلب، هو بعينه القادح المسمى في الأصول بالقلب، وضابطه عند الأصوليين أن يُثبت المعترض نقيض حكم المستدل بعين دليل المستدل فيقلب دليله عليه لا له . والمستدل في الأصول هو المعلل في البحث والمناظرة، والمعترض في الأصول هو السائل في البحث والمناظرة، فتذكر اختلاف الاصطلاحين ؛ لئلا يلتبس عليك المقصود بغيره . وإذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن القلب عند أهل الأصول قسمان : أحدهما ما صحح فيه المعترض مذهبه ، وذلك التصحيح فيه إبطال مذهب خصمه، سواء كان مذهب الخصم المستدل مصرحًا به في دليله أو لا . ومثال ما كان مصرحًا به فيه قول الشافعي في بيع الفضولي : عقد في حق الغير بلا ولاية عليه فلا يصح قياسًا على شراء الفضولي ؛ فإنه يصح لمن سمّاه . فيقول المعترض كالمالكي والحنفي : عقد فيصح كشراء الفضولي ؛ فإنه يصح لمن سمّاه إذا رضي ذلك المسمّى له، وإلا لزم الفضولي ، فهو صحيح على كل حال . ومثال غير المصرح به فيه قول من يشترط الصوم في الاعتكاف
Chapter on Opposition and its Types. Know that what is called in research and debate 'opposition' (mu'arada) by way of reversal (qalb) is exactly the objection (qadih) called in usul 'qalb' (reversal). Its rule according to usul scholars is that the objector (mu'tarid) establishes the opposite of the claimant's (mustadill) ruling using the very same evidence of the claimant, thus turning his evidence against him, not for him. The 'mustadill' in usul is the reasoner (mu'allil) in research and debate, and the 'mu'tarid' in usul is the questioner (sa'il) in research and debate. So remember the difference in terminology lest the intended meaning be confused with something else. And if you know that, then know that qalb according to the people of usul is of two types: One type is where the objector vindicates his own school (madhhab), and that vindication entails invalidating the school of his opponent, whether the opponent's (the mustadill) school is explicitly stated in his evidence or not. An example of what is explicitly stated: the saying of al-Shafi'i regarding the sale by an unauthorized person (bay' al-fuduli): 'A contract concerning the right of another without authority over it, so it is invalid, by analogy to the purchase by an unauthorized person, for it is valid for the one whom he named.' So the objector, such as the Maliki or Hanafi, says: 'It is a contract, so it is valid, like the purchase by an unauthorized person, for it is valid for the one whom he named if that named person consents, otherwise it is binding on the unauthorized person, so it is valid in any case.' And an example of what is not explicitly stated: the saying of one who stipulates fasting in i'tikaf...
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is from a new chapter on Mu'arada (Opposition) and its types, specifically focusing on Qalb (Reversal). The author defines mu'arada as qalb: the objector uses the same evidence as the claimant to prove the opposite conclusion. He clarifies terminology differences between usul al-fiqh and dialectic (bahth wa-munazara): the 'mustadill' (claimant) is called 'mu'allil' (reasoner) in dialectic, and the 'mu'tarid' (objector) is called 'sa'il' (questioner). The author then splits qalb into two divisions based on whether the opponent's school is explicitly stated in his evidence. He gives a complete example from Shafi'i's ruling on bay' al-fuduli: Shafi'i argues it is invalid by analogy to the purchase by an unauthorized person (which is valid for the named person but invalid if not accepted). The objector counters: it is a contract, so it is valid, because the same evidence shows the purchase is valid for the named person upon consent, and if not, it is binding on the unauthorized person, so the sale is valid in all cases. This shows how qalb works: the same analogy is used to derive the opposite ruling. A second example (regarding fasting in i'tikaf) is begun but cut off.

Key Takeaway
Qalb is a dialectical technique where the objector uses the claimant's own evidence to derive the opposite ruling, turning the proof against the claimant.

What is Qalb? The Core Concept
Definition of qalb and its basic mechanism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qalb (inversion) and identify its key feature: using the opponent's proof to derive the opposite conclusion.

Core Idea
Qalb turns the opponent's proof against him, not for him.

This passage from the textbook on dialectic introduces a powerful objection technique: inversion (qalb). Rather than bringing new evidence, the objector uses the proponent's own proof to disprove his claim. Understanding qalb is essential for engaging in high-level jadal and usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two roles
The passage defines the proponent (mustadill) and objector (mu'tarid). Pay attention to the terminological shift between usul and debate (jadal).
2
Understand the mechanism of qalb
The objector does not bring a new proof; he takes the proponent's proof and shows that it leads to the opposite ruling. This is the defining feature of qalb.
3
Spot the key phrase
The author says 'يقلب دليله عليه لا له' (turns his proof against him, not for him). This captures the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعارضة وأقسامها
اعلم أن ما يسمى في البحث والمناظرة بالمعارضة على سبيل القلب، هو بعينه القادح المسمى في الأصول بالقلب، وضابطه عند الأصوليين أن يُثبت المعترض نقيض حكم المستدل بعين دليل المستدل فيقلب دليله عليه لا له.
والمستدل في الأصول هو المعلل في البحث والمناظرة، والمعترض في الأصول هو السائل في البحث والمناظرة، فتذكر اختلاف الاصطلاحين؛ لئلا يلتبس عليك المقصود بغيره.
وإذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن القلب عند أهل الأصول قسمان:
أحدهما ما صحح فيه المعترض مذهبه، وذلك التصحيح فيه إبطال مذهب خصمه، سواء كان مذهب الخصم المستدل مصرحًا به في دليله أو لا.
ومثال ما كان مصرحًا به فيه قول الشافعي في بيع الفضولي: عقد في حق الغير بلا ولاية عليه فلا يصح قياسًا على شراء الفضولي؛ فإنه يصح لمن سمّاه.
فيقول المعترض كالمالكي والحنفي: عقد فيصح كشراء الفضولي؛ فإنه يصح لمن سمّاه إذا رضي ذلك المسمّى له، وإلا لزم الفضولي، فهو صحيح على كل حال.
ومثال غير المصرح به فيه قول من يشترط الصوم في الاعتكاف كالمالكي: الاعتكاف لبث فلا يكون قربة بنفسه، كوقوف عرفة، أي فإنه قربة بضميمة الإحرام إليه، فكذلك الاعتكاف إنما يكون قربة بضميمة عبادة إليه، وهي الصوم في الاعتكاف المتنازع فيه، ومذهبه وهو اشتراط الصوم في الاعتكاف غير مصرح به في دليله.
فيقول المعترض كالشافعي: الاعتكاف لبث فلا يشترط فيه الصوم، كوقوف عرفة، أي فإنه لا يُشترط فيه الصوم.
القسم الثاني من قسمي القلب هو ما كان لإبطال مذهب الخصم من غير تعرض لتصحيح مذهب المعترض، سواء كان الإبطال المذكور مدلولاً عليه بالمطابقة أو الالتزام.
ومثال كون الإبطال مدلولا عليه بالمطابقة قول الحنفي في مسح الرأس: عضو وضوء فلا يكفي في مسحه أقل ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح؛ قياسًا على الوجه؛ فإنه لا يكفي في غسله ذلك.
فيقول المعترض كالشافعي: عضو وضوء فلا يقدر بالربع؛ كالوجه؛ فإن غسله لا يتقدر بالربع.
ومثال الإبطال بالالتزام قول الحنفي في جواز بيع الغائب: عقد معاوضة فيصح مع الجهل بالعوض؛ كالنكاح؛ فإنه يصح مع الجهل بالزوجة، أي عدم رؤيتها.
فيقول المعترض كالمالكي: فلا يثبت فيه خيار الرؤية كالنكاح. فقد أبطل مذهب الحنفي بالالتزام؛ لأن ثبوت خيار الرؤية لازم
Chapter on opposition and its divisions. Know that what is called in investigation and debate 'opposition by way of inversion' is precisely the same as the objection called 'inversion' in principles [of jurisprudence]. Its definition according to the scholars of principles is that the objector establishes the opposite of the proponent's ruling using the very same proof of the proponent, thus turning his proof against him, not for him. The proponent in principles is the reasoner in investigation and debate, and the objector in principles is the questioner in investigation and debate. So be mindful of the difference between the two terminologies, lest the intended meaning be confused with something else. Once you know this, know that inversion according to the scholars of principles is of two types: First: that in which the objector validates his own doctrine, and that validation therein invalidates the doctrine of his opponent, whether the opponent's doctrine is explicitly stated in his proof or not. An example of what is explicitly stated therein is al-Shafi'i's statement regarding the sale by an unauthorized person: 'A contract regarding the right of another without authority over him, so it is invalid, by analogy to the purchase by an unauthorized person; for it is valid for the one he named.' The objector, such as the Maliki and Hanafi, says: 'A contract, so it is valid like the purchase by an unauthorized person; for it is valid for the one he named if that named one consents, otherwise it is binding on the unauthorized person, so it is valid in any case.' An example of what is not explicitly stated therein is the statement of one who requires fasting in i'tikaf, like the Maliki: 'I'tikaf is staying, so it is not an act of worship in itself, like standing at 'Arafah; for it is an act of worship by attaching ihram to it, so likewise i'tikaf is only an act of worship by attaching an act of worship to it, namely fasting in the disputed i'tikaf' – and his doctrine, which is requiring fasting in i'tikaf, is not explicitly stated in his proof. The objector, like al-Shafi'i, says: 'I'tikaf is staying, so fasting is not required in it, like standing at 'Arafah; for fasting is not required in it.' The second type of the two types of inversion is that which is used to invalidate the opponent's doctrine without attempting to validate the objector's own doctrine, whether that invalidation is indicated by direct implication or by required implication. An example of invalidation indicated by direct implication is the Hanafi's statement regarding wiping the head: 'A limb of ablution, so the least that the name of wiping applies to is not sufficient for its wiping, by analogy to the face; for that is not sufficient in its washing.' The objector, like al-Shafi'i, says: 'A limb of ablution, so it is not limited to a quarter, like the face; for its washing is not limited to a quarter.' An example of invalidation by required implication is the Hanafi's statement regarding the permissibility of selling an absent object: 'A contract of exchange, so it is valid despite ignorance of the consideration, like marriage; for it is valid despite ignorance of the wife, i.e., not seeing her.' The objector, like the Maliki, says: 'Then the option of seeing is not established in it, like marriage.' Thus he invalidated the Hanafi's doctrine by required implication, because the establishment of the option of seeing is a necessary implication,'.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of mu'arada (opposition) in the context of jadal (dialectical debate), specifically the form known as qalb (inversion). The author first clarifies that what debaters call 'opposition by way of inversion' is exactly the same as the 'qadih' (objection) termed 'qalb' in usul al-fiqh (principles of jurisprudence). The definition is crucial: the objector (mu'tarid) establishes the opposite of the proponent's (mustadill) ruling using the very same proof (dalil) the proponent used, thus turning the proof against him. The author warns of terminological differences: in usul, the proponent is called mustadill, the objector mu'tarid; in debate, they are mu'allil (reasoner) and sa'il (questioner). This sets the stage.

The author then divides qalb into two types. The first type is where the objector inverts the proof not only to refute the opponent but also to establish his own doctrine (madhhab). This type includes two subcases: (a) when the opponent's doctrine is explicitly stated in his proof, and (b) when it is not. The example for (a) is the dispute over the sale by a fuduli (unauthorized person) between al-Shafi'i and the Malikis/Hanafis. Al-Shafi'i's proof is that a contract on another's right without authority is invalid, analogizing to the purchase by the same person (which is valid only for the named party). The objector turns the analogy: if the purchase is valid in some cases (with consent), then the sale can also be valid. The example for (b) is the requirement of fasting in i'tikaf. The Maliki argues that i'tikaf is like staying at 'Arafah, which becomes worship only with ihram, so i'tikaf needs fasting. The Shafi'i objector turns the same premise: since staying at 'Arafah does not require fasting, i'tikaf does not need it either.

The second type of qalb aims solely to invalidate the opponent's doctrine without establishing one's own. This invalidation can be either by direct implication (dalala mutabaqa) or by required implication (dalala iltizam). The direct implication example: the Hanafi says wiping the head, as an ablution limb, does not require the least amount, analogizing to the face which does not require the least washing. The objector says: being an ablution limb does not necessitate a quarter measurement, like the face (which has no minimum). The required implication example: the Hanafi argues that selling an absent object is valid because it is an exchange contract like marriage, which is valid despite ignorance of the spouse. The objector says: then the option of seeing (khiyar al-ru'ya) should not apply, but in practice it does, so the analogy fails. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the examples are complete.

Key Terms
المعارضة
القلب
المستدل
المعترض

C) The objector questions the validity of the proponent's premises.
D) The objector refuses to engage with the proponent's argument.

Key Takeaway
Qalb is a dialectical move where the objector uses the opponent's proof to derive the opposite ruling.

The First Type of Qalb: Establishing One's Own Doctrine
First type of qalb: establishing one's own doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the first type of qalb, where the objector invalidates the opponent and establishes his own doctrine, using the examples from the text.

Core Idea
The first type of qalb not only refutes but also validates the objector's own madhhab.

The passage divides qalb into two types. The first type is more complete: the objector turns the proof to both refute the opponent and establish his own position. This is illustrated with two examples from fiqh: the sale by an unauthorized person and the requirement of fasting in i'tikaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subcases
The first type has two subcases: (a) the opponent's doctrine is explicitly in the proof, (b) it is not explicitly stated. Find these in the text.
2
Analyze the fuduli example
Al-Shafi'i uses an analogy to prove the sale is invalid. The objector uses the same analogy to prove it is valid, thereby establishing the Hanafi/Maliki position.
3
Analyze the i'tikaf example
The Maliki requires fasting in i'tikaf. The Shafi'i objector uses the same analogy (i'tikaf is like staying at 'Arafah) to show that fasting is not required, establishing the Shafi'i position.

فصل في المعارضة وأقسامها
اعلم أن ما يسمى في البحث والمناظرة بالمعارضة على سبيل القلب، هو بعينه القادح المسمى في الأصول بالقلب، وضابطه عند الأصوليين أن يُثبت المعترض نقيض حكم المستدل بعين دليل المستدل فيقلب دليله عليه لا له.
والمستدل في الأصول هو المعلل في البحث والمناظرة، والمعترض في الأصول هو السائل في البحث والمناظرة، فتذكر اختلاف الاصطلاحين؛ لئلا يلتبس عليك المقصود بغيره.
وإذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن القلب عند أهل الأصول قسمان:
أحدهما ما صحح فيه المعترض مذهبه، وذلك التصحيح فيه إبطال مذهب خصمه، سواء كان مذهب الخصم المستدل مصرحًا به في دليله أو لا.
ومثال ما كان مصرحًا به فيه قول الشافعي في بيع الفضولي: عقد في حق الغير بلا ولاية عليه فلا يصح قياسًا على شراء الفضولي؛ فإنه يصح لمن سمّاه.
فيقول المعترض كالمالكي والحنفي: عقد فيصح كشراء الفضولي؛ فإنه يصح لمن سمّاه إذا رضي ذلك المسمّى له، وإلا لزم الفضولي، فهو صحيح على كل حال.
ومثال غير المصرح به فيه قول من يشترط الصوم في الاعتكاف كالمالكي: الاعتكاف لبث فلا يكون قربة بنفسه، كوقوف عرفة، أي فإنه قربة بضميمة الإحرام إليه، فكذلك الاعتكاف إنما يكون قربة بضميمة عبادة إليه، وهي الصوم في الاعتكاف المتنازع فيه، ومذهبه وهو اشتراط الصوم في الاعتكاف غير مصرح به في دليله.
فيقول المعترض كالشافعي: الاعتكاف لبث فلا يشترط فيه الصوم، كوقوف عرفة، أي فإنه لا يُشترط فيه الصوم.
القسم الثاني من قسمي القلب هو ما كان لإبطال مذهب الخصم من غير تعرض لتصحيح مذهب المعترض، سواء كان الإبطال المذكور مدلولاً عليه بالمطابقة أو الالتزام.
ومثال كون الإبطال مدلولا عليه بالمطابقة قول الحنفي في مسح الرأس: عضو وضوء فلا يكفي في مسحه أقل ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح؛ قياسًا على الوجه؛ فإنه لا يكفي في غسله ذلك.
فيقول المعترض كالشافعي: عضو وضوء فلا يقدر بالربع؛ كالوجه؛ فإن غسله لا يتقدر بالربع.
ومثال الإبطال بالالتزام قول الحنفي في جواز بيع الغائب: عقد معاوضة فيصح مع الجهل بالعوض؛ كالنكاح؛ فإنه يصح مع الجهل بالزوجة، أي عدم رؤيتها.
فيقول المعترض كالمالكي: فلا يثبت فيه خيار الرؤية كالنكاح. فقد أبطل مذهب الحنفي بالالتزام؛ لأن ثبوت خيار الرؤية لازم
Chapter on opposition and its divisions. Know that what is called in investigation and debate 'opposition by way of inversion' is precisely the same as the objection called 'inversion' in principles [of jurisprudence]. Its definition according to the scholars of principles is that the objector establishes the opposite of the proponent's ruling using the very same proof of the proponent, thus turning his proof against him, not for him. The proponent in principles is the reasoner in investigation and debate, and the objector in principles is the questioner in investigation and debate. So be mindful of the difference between the two terminologies, lest the intended meaning be confused with something else. Once you know this, know that inversion according to the scholars of principles is of two types: First: that in which the objector validates his own doctrine, and that validation therein invalidates the doctrine of his opponent, whether the opponent's doctrine is explicitly stated in his proof or not. An example of what is explicitly stated therein is al-Shafi'i's statement regarding the sale by an unauthorized person: 'A contract regarding the right of another without authority over him, so it is invalid, by analogy to the purchase by an unauthorized person; for it is valid for the one he named.' The objector, such as the Maliki and Hanafi, says: 'A contract, so it is valid like the purchase by an unauthorized person; for it is valid for the one he named if that named one consents, otherwise it is binding on the unauthorized person, so it is valid in any case.' An example of what is not explicitly stated therein is the statement of one who requires fasting in i'tikaf, like the Maliki: 'I'tikaf is staying, so it is not an act of worship in itself, like standing at 'Arafah; for it is an act of worship by attaching ihram to it, so likewise i'tikaf is only an act of worship by attaching an act of worship to it, namely fasting in the disputed i'tikaf' – and his doctrine, which is requiring fasting in i'tikaf, is not explicitly stated in his proof. The objector, like al-Shafi'i, says: 'I'tikaf is staying, so fasting is not required in it, like standing at 'Arafah; for fasting is not required in it.' The second type of the two types of inversion is that which is used to invalidate the opponent's doctrine without attempting to validate the objector's own doctrine, whether that invalidation is indicated by direct implication or by required implication. An example of invalidation indicated by direct implication is the Hanafi's statement regarding wiping the head: 'A limb of ablution, so the least that the name of wiping applies to is not sufficient for its wiping, by analogy to the face; for that is not sufficient in its washing.' The objector, like al-Shafi'i, says: 'A limb of ablution, so it is not limited to a quarter, like the face; for its washing is not limited to a quarter.' An example of invalidation by required implication is the Hanafi's statement regarding the permissibility of selling an absent object: 'A contract of exchange, so it is valid despite ignorance of the consideration, like marriage; for it is valid despite ignorance of the wife, i.e., not seeing her.' The objector, like the Maliki, says: 'Then the option of seeing is not established in it, like marriage.' Thus he invalidated the Hanafi's doctrine by required implication, because the establishment of the option of seeing is a necessary implication,'.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of mu'arada (opposition) in the context of jadal (dialectical debate), specifically the form known as qalb (inversion). The author first clarifies that what debaters call 'opposition by way of inversion' is exactly the same as the 'qadih' (objection) termed 'qalb' in usul al-fiqh (principles of jurisprudence). The definition is crucial: the objector (mu'tarid) establishes the opposite of the proponent's (mustadill) ruling using the very same proof (dalil) the proponent used, thus turning the proof against him. The author warns of terminological differences: in usul, the proponent is called mustadill, the objector mu'tarid; in debate, they are mu'allil (reasoner) and sa'il (questioner). This sets the stage.

The author then divides qalb into two types. The first type is where the objector inverts the proof not only to refute the opponent but also to establish his own doctrine (madhhab). This type includes two subcases: (a) when the opponent's doctrine is explicitly stated in his proof, and (b) when it is not. The example for (a) is the dispute over the sale by a fuduli (unauthorized person) between al-Shafi'i and the Malikis/Hanafis. Al-Shafi'i's proof is that a contract on another's right without authority is invalid, analogizing to the purchase by the same person (which is valid only for the named party). The objector turns the analogy: if the purchase is valid in some cases (with consent), then the sale can also be valid. The example for (b) is the requirement of fasting in i'tikaf. The Maliki argues that i'tikaf is like staying at 'Arafah, which becomes worship only with ihram, so i'tikaf needs fasting. The Shafi'i objector turns the same premise: since staying at 'Arafah does not require fasting, i'tikaf does not need it either.

The second type of qalb aims solely to invalidate the opponent's doctrine without establishing one's own. This invalidation can be either by direct implication (dalala mutabaqa) or by required implication (dalala iltizam). The direct implication example: the Hanafi says wiping the head, as an ablution limb, does not require the least amount, analogizing to the face which does not require the least washing. The objector says: being an ablution limb does not necessitate a quarter measurement, like the face (which has no minimum). The required implication example: the Hanafi argues that selling an absent object is valid because it is an exchange contract like marriage, which is valid despite ignorance of the spouse. The objector says: then the option of seeing (khiyar al-ru'ya) should not apply, but in practice it does, so the analogy fails. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the examples are complete.

Key Terms
القلب الأول
المستدل
المعترض
البيع الفضولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fuduli example, what does the objector aim to do?
A) Only invalidate al-Shafi'i's ruling.
B) Establish his own madhhab by using the same analogy.
C) Point out a flaw in the analogy.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The first type of qalb validates the objector's own madhhab while invalidating the opponent's.

?
Construct a similar qalb argument on a different fiqh issue, such as the permissibility of salam sale. Use a known analogy and invert it.

The Second Type of Qalb: Direct and Required Implication
Second type of qalb: modes of invalidation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between qalb by direct implication (mutabaqa) and by required implication (iltizam), using the examples from the text.

Core Idea
The second type of qalb only invalidates the opponent, and it does so either directly from the proof's wording or from a necessary consequence.

The second type of qalb does not seek to establish the objector's own position; it simply aims to refute the opponent. The passage distinguishes two ways this can be done: by direct implication (the conclusion follows immediately from the opponent's statement) or by required implication (it follows from a necessary consequence of the opponent's statement).

Guided Path
1
Understand the two modes
Direct implication (mutabaqa) means the invalidation is directly derived from the opponent's words. Required implication (iltizam) means it is derived from a necessary consequence of those words.
2
Analyze the wiping the head example
The Hanafi analogizes wiping the head to washing the face, concluding that the minimum is not sufficient. The objector uses the same premise to conclude that the measurement is not limited to a quarter, which directly contradicts the Hanafi's implicit requirement.
This is direct implication.
3
Analyze the selling absent object example
The Hanafi analogizes selling an absent object to marriage, arguing ignorance of consideration is allowed. The objector points out a necessary implication: if it's like marriage, then the option of seeing should not apply, but it does, thus invalidating the analogy.
This is required implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعارضة وأقسامها
اعلم أن ما يسمى في البحث والمناظرة بالمعارضة على سبيل القلب، هو بعينه القادح المسمى في الأصول بالقلب، وضابطه عند الأصوليين أن يُثبت المعترض نقيض حكم المستدل بعين دليل المستدل فيقلب دليله عليه لا له.
والمستدل في الأصول هو المعلل في البحث والمناظرة، والمعترض في الأصول هو السائل في البحث والمناظرة، فتذكر اختلاف الاصطلاحين؛ لئلا يلتبس عليك المقصود بغيره.
وإذا علمت ذلك فاعلم أن القلب عند أهل الأصول قسمان:
أحدهما ما صحح فيه المعترض مذهبه، وذلك التصحيح فيه إبطال مذهب خصمه، سواء كان مذهب الخصم المستدل مصرحًا به في دليله أو لا.
ومثال ما كان مصرحًا به فيه قول الشافعي في بيع الفضولي: عقد في حق الغير بلا ولاية عليه فلا يصح قياسًا على شراء الفضولي؛ فإنه يصح لمن سمّاه.

intermediate
